Tlx© Influenza Fiend!
For such must be considered the living: organism which .by its rapid, propagation has spread disease and death
throughout the limits of the civilised world. The scientists of the period have attributed the prevalence and
transmission of this influenza epidemic to the action of quickly propagating germs, and although keen-eyed and
learned men in England, France and Germany have been searching for this microscopic mischief with unstinting
labour, they have not yet succeeded in isolating the individual germ and learning his habits, his food and his
poison. In the meantime, with no Uncertain note, leading physicians in London have recognised in the Austra-
lian Oil of Eucalyptus a germicide that has been pronounced both a preventive of and a cure tor Influenza.
In the Lancet of 18th January, 1890, Dr. W. Stephenson Richmond, of Kensington, London, writes;—
“As to the treatment, preventive and curative, I believe enough has not been said about the former.
Believing influenza to be due to a germ, and generally preceded by catarrh, I assume preventive medioine-should
aim at destruction of the germ and relief of the congestion. I believe for the former there is nothing to surpass
eucalyptus oH during the present epidemic. I believe it is absolutely preventive, and my experience and observa-
tion of it are not confined to the present epidemic. Eucalyptus destroys the germs,.and added to ammonia, which
stimulates the local circulation, forms the best inhalent for the prevention of the disease I yet know of. I thihk
it vastly superior to menthol.”—(Signed), W. Stephenson Richmond, M.R.C.S.
In February, 1890, Dr. Brendon Curgenven writes in the Lancet.
To the Editor of the Lancet : “ I can fully endorse the experience of Mr. Stephenson Richmond in the use of
Eucalyptus as a preventive of influenza, as recommended by him in the Lancet of 18th January. I have from the
beginning of the epidemic used Eucalyptus disinfectant spray on the handkerchief as a perfume by day, and
sprinkled on the bed pillow for inhalation at night, and have found it most effectual in preventing influenza. In
the cases of old people and invalids, I have directed the spray to be used in their rooms at intervals in the day,
and sprinkled on their pillows at night, and I am convinced I have saved many from the disease, when their
servants, who have not taken these precautions, liave had it. I have not had the disease myself, although I have
attended upwards of 200 cases. I have experienced the effects of a large dose of the poison several times after
visiting houses where there were many cases. I felt considerable nausea, like that of sea-sickness, and such dizzi-
ness that I could scarcely walk straight, together with great languor. All these symptoms passed away on In-
haling the Eucalyptus with strong inspirations until its warmth could be felt in the chest. It will as effectually
destroy the poisons of other inf scions diseases and fevers, and I hope to bring the subject of the use of eucalyptus
in infeetious diseases before the Epidemiological Society on 12th March, when I shall prove its powerful effect on
the scarlet fever poison.” — l am, sir, yours faithfully, (Signed), J. Brendon Chroe'nven, Bayswater, Ist February,
1890.
Bosisto’s “ Parrot,” Brand is both curative and preventive. Its antiseptic principle immedi-
ately destroys all poisonous germs. v
Mr. Joseph Bosisto was the first to distil and introduce eucalyptus oil into Europe, and his oil may be known
by the trada-fharg, the “ Parrot” brand. He first exhibited his eucalyptus oil at the Paris Exhibition of 1855,
then again in the London Exhibition of 1861, receiving the highest honours for his exhibits. In 1886, the Society
of Arts, Manufactures, and Commerce hf London (incorporated by Royal Charter in 1847, and established in 1754)
conferred on Mr. Bosisto the silver medal of the society in recognition of his service* ‘to medicine and the arts hi
introducing the eucalyptus of the Australian flora into the commerce of Eifrope.
From the earliest years of its introduction into Europe Bosisto’* oil of eucalyptus has filled the European
demand, and will he found in the dispensaries-of all the hospitals, and in the laboratories erf all the leading
chemists of Europe.
Bosisto’s “ PARROT ” brand Eucalyptus Oil has as trade mark a parrot on the top of the wrapper. See yon
get this from your chemist, and so avoid imitations.

BUCKINGHAM PARK ESTATE,
MEDBOW (Blue Mountains).
This charming Estate has its Railway Platform almost
adjoining, and its level land proves it to be eminently
adapted for residential purposes. Nature seems to have
lavished all her varied charms on BUCKINGHAM PARK,
Which is, without exception, the CREAM or PICKED
SPOT on the BLUE MOUNTAINS.
BUCKINGHAM PARK ESTATE
Is beautifully surveyed. The Allotments are unusually
large—from 100 up to 180 feet frontages, with long depths
—thus enabling buyers to have Garden, Tennis Lawns,
etc., if they so desire. In fact, it may be truly said, there
is no finer Residential Land on the Mountains.
The BELGRAVIA HOTEL, MBDLOW, which cost £4OOO,
is now open for visitors, and is the First Hotel on the
Mountains supplied with the Electric Light, and the
Proprietor is willing to supply any cottages built on the
estate with the electric light. Two elegant Villas are
now nearly finished on this estate. The rapid advance in
the price of land at BLACKHEATH during the past three
years, amounting to fully 300 per cent., clearly shows that
early buyers at Buckingham Park will make a splendid
investment. Title is TORRENS, and the terms are liberal
and to suit buyers. For particulars, apply to the Pro-
prietor,

A FRESH FIELD FOR THE SOCIAL GIMLETS.
“The suffrage once fairly attained for women, thei first work must be to amend the marriage laws, or, blasphemous as the words may sound, do away with them
altogether... No woman, until she has known'love and achieved the dignity of motherhood can be thoroughly complete.”— Mrs. J. It Ashton, before N.S.W.
Woman Suffrage League.

The Editor will carefully read and acknowledge in the
"Correspondence” column all contributions submitted — 1
whether in the form of Political, Social, or other Articles,
Verse, Short Tales or Sketches (those dealing with Aus-
tralian subjects, and not exceeding two columns in
length, or say 3000 words, are specially acceptable),
Paragraphs, Letters, or Newspaper-Clippings. All
communications will be regarded as strictly confidential.

the address is specified and stamps are enclosed. But sue
desire those sending MSS. to distinctly understand that
we are not responsible for the preservation or transmission
of such MSS.

SATURDAY, NOVEMBER 21, 1891.
“ The Multitude of Counsellors.”
A few days ago a short, thick-set, white-
headed, resolute individual named Stanley
arrived in Australia, and proceeded to deliver
a series of lectures concerning how he found
Livingstone. Along with this information
he also proceeded, almost on the first day of
his sojourn in this country, to advise the
Australian people as to the best means of con-
structinga new nation, and the correctmanner
in which to run a newly-settled continent,
flis qualifications for this work consisted
chiefly in the fact that he had done much
walking and had humped much ponderous
luggage in Central Africa—that lie had set out
in the morning from a dirty village of squalid
niggers, and tramped dismally all" day
through slush and forest, and arrived in the
evening at a squalid village of dirty niggers
also, that he had shot some black men, and
flogged some more. It was because of these
things that he started to counsel a strange
people how to run their own strange world,
and by reason of the squalid niggers, and
the dirty villages, and the luggage, and the
fact that he found Livingstone, his opinions
will doubtless be received with respect.
Almost on the same day another distinguished
visitor issued his ukase, also, as to what
the Australian nation ought to do
with itself, and despite the fact that
his views were widely different from
those of the other, they, too, will
doubtless be listened to with a certain
amount of veneration. The second man’s
capacity for giving the “straight tip ’’ was
based on the fact that he was learned in the
matter of the alleys and slums of a land
16,000 miles distant—that he was well up in
dog-holes and refuse and pestilence—and that
he was a recognised authority on gaols and
workhouses ; and out of the abundance of his
knowledge on these subjects he also rose to
inform this country that he was willing to be
a father and counsellor to it, and to see it
through. Both of them started, apparently,
on the assumption that Australia knew
nothing of its own affairs, and that it badly
needed the preacher from the by-ways of
London, and the explorer from the forests
of Africa, to put it right, and keep an eye
upon things generally. As all “ distinguished
visitors ” —whether they be distinguished
soldiers or prominent aeronauts or leading
horsey men start to superintend the
government of Australia in precisely the
same way, this circumstance is by no means
surprising.
• # #
We will give the first place to the Rev.
William Booth— self-styled a “General”
to gratify his own innate craving after titles,
however shoddy—clad in glaring red, like a
long and badly-painted Mephistopheles—
autocratic, with a hawk-like eye for the cash,
and a red and varnished nose that sniffs
unceasingly after portable assets. And
this is what he says is the great present
necessity of Australia :
My opinion is that what the colonies want is a class
Similar to the agricultural class in England, men who will
be quite satisfied it they have plenty to eat and drink,
are comfortably clad, and can make some provision for
old age. That is what is wanted here. A class of
peasantry—people who, if they get a cottage and cow and
a horse, and plenty to eat and drink, will be content.
In other words, what Australia wants,
according to the views of Booth, is a class
of people without brains, or knowledge, or any
thinking capacity whatever. They must be
placid and solemn and cow-like, and have no
aspirations beyond a cottage and two animals.
And as the only possible means of prevent-
ing ambition, they must have no education,
for education leads to ambition, and the
only man who is absolutely content with
two beasts and a roof is the one who knows of
nothing beyond. The sample peasant must
have no literature beyond a Bible and a
hymn-book, and possibly some solemn publi-
cation about agriculture, and a War Cry.
He should be able to read these only with
extreme - difficulty, because if he reads
fluently he may drift into literature which
will call up visions of a wider and grander
world than that which is bounded by his
hovel and his domestic mule. He shouldn’t
vote—in fact, he should be too ignorant
to know how to vote, for a man who
can take an intelligent interest in poli-
tics is liable to grow dissatisfied, and
some day he may abandon his shanty
and his animals, and become a statesman.
He should be slow, dull, hog-like, large-
footed, stupid, and horny ; he should have
his religion supplied ready-made by the Rev.
William Booth, and he should be so heavy
and illiterate that he will do all his life
what William Booth tells him to do, and
be unable to reason for himself. He should,
in short, be simply a huge, sodden, and pig-
headed infant, and until Australia lays in a
large quantity of him things will never go
right in the Fifth Continent. This is the
advice of the Salvation autocrat, and as he
lives by bossing the mental monstrosity
which he describes, it is just the sort of
advice which might reasonably be expected
rom such a quarter.
* • #
H. M. Stanley, the homicidal explorer
from Africa, has a different inspiration, and
plans out for Australia an entirely different
future. His thoughts, naturally enough,
run chiefly upon niggers. In his view,
apparently, no country is complete without
its nigger, and the chief use and purpose of
the white man is to boss the degraded black
individual and keep him up to his work.
Therefore, where the black man is not, there
the white man is a person without a voca-
tion. He proposes, in consequence, that a
“ colour line ” should be drawn across
Australia from East to West, somewhere
above the tropic of Capricorn, and on
the Southern side of that line the
dusky heathen should be absolutely pro-
hibited. On the other side, however, he
should be grown and cultivated as a profit-
able institution, so that the white man may
use him, and make money out of him, and
have a high old time generally. Considering
how exceedingly abundant the murky
brother is in the adjacent land of Asia, it is
more than probable, under this regime, that
the coloured population of tropical Australia
would by-and-bye far exceed the white popu-
lation of the southern provinces. Then
there would grow up a race of nigger-drivers,
overseers, and gentlemen planters, who
would regard manual labour as something
beneath the dignity of the Aryan ; and along
with them would arise a degraded class of
“ mean whites”-—individuals who were
unable to find billets as nigger-drivers, and
who loafed because work was too degrading
for a white man’s dignity. Then the white
man of the North would learn to despise the
white man of the South, because the latter
did his own toil and had no nigger ; and the
man of the South would hate the one of the
North because of the undisguised contempt
with which he was regarded ; and the Aus-
tralian people would branch off into two hos-
tile races that would diverge more and more
widely as the years went on. Then, too, it
would require a whole army of soldiers or
police to guard the line of demarcation, and
keep the black population within its own
boundary, and the proceedings would end,
most probably, with a servile insurrection,
a racial war, and general chaos. All these
risks are to be run for the mere joy and
pleasure ancj privilege of having a nigger,
and the “distinguished visitor” who drew
up this scheme of elaborate foolishness did
so, apparently, merely because he had learned
to.look upon the owning of niggers as the
chief purpose of the white man’s existence.
If his life had been spent in owning a
menagerie, he would probably have been as
firmly convinced that the business of every
man’s life was to own a tiger and a big
rhinoceros, and finding that the Australian
had no tiger or rhinoceros, he would have
drawn up plans and specifications for growing
these animals with the same deadly solemnity,
and with an equally profound conviction that
no nation was complete without them.
* # «
One or two other individuals of more or
less importance have various other varieties
of advice to offer. Governor Kintore of
South Australia—celebrated chiefly as the
most reckless amateur who ever drove an
engine, and as a luxurious explorer who left
a track of champagne-bottles and corks all the
way along the made-track across this continent
—desires to devote the far North to the grmvtli
and cultivation of Chinamen. He regards
the Chow as a hardy cereal who will flourish
in all kinds of exposed situations, and he
seems to look with a pitying eye upon the
Australians as a nation that is short of
Chinamen. Also a certain Mr. Horn, of the
South Australian Legislature, wishes to plant
the coolie promiscuously all over the North,
and has introduced a motion to that effect.
All these individuals are travelling on the
one theory that this country has vast areas
of land which need to be sold without the
slightest delay, and dug up, and planted—
that it has great mineral deposits that need
to be exploited instantly—that it has pearl
fisheries which ought to be rushed imme-
diately, and forests which need to be cut
down and turned into cash in one act. And
because a local supply of white men can’t be
got fast enough, so that the heritage of the
Australian nation may be wasted and used up
right off, Kintore desires that it shall be
given away to Chinamen, and Stanley longs
to throw it to imported niggers, and Booth
wants to hand it over to a dreary and pig-
like race of clods from England, whose one
recommendation is that they are to have no
brains, no ambition, no knowledge, and none
of the attributes of man beyond a dull,
solemn, animal yearning for a roof and a
horse. They are all resolved, too, that Aus-
tralia wants much good advice and super-
vision, and each of them is willing to act as
a kind of dismal and fat-headed Uncle
Pumblechook to this orphaned land, and to
see it through generally.
* * *
The cry of the “distinguished visitor,”
and of many other visitors who are not dis-
tinguished, and of many people who are not
visitors, is that Australia wants population.
It is universally taken for granted by these
officious advisers that Australia wants pojiu-
lation so badly that if no other varieties are
to be had it would sooner have Chinamen
and niggers than go without. And the
average “ visitor ” is so hopelessly consumed
by this one theory that it seems of but little
use to tell Kintore and Booth and similar
public nuisances that Australia is not pining
after population of any sort or variety
whatever. When Kintore points out how
easily a large stock of Mongols could be
planted in the North, he strikes no more
responsive a chord than if he drew attention
to the ease with which an immense consign-
ment of boa-constrictors could be turned
loose on the same spot. When Booth states
that Australia’s one great necessity is a huge
stock of his own particular brand of soulless
British peasant, he might as reasonably and
as successfully appeal to his hearers on the
ground that they wanted a new supply of
green bugs. And when other men from
Africa or elsewhere call attention to the
urgent need of nigger-labour, they fall flat
for exactly the same reason - they might as
well call attention to the need for an imme-
diate stock of polecats. For the distin-
guished visitor always travels on the
assumption that what Australia needs is
men, while the Australian has already
decided that, except for purposes of defence,
it matters not at all whether Australia ever
possesses more men than it does at
present. The reason of this is sufficiently
simple. All over Europe and Asia, and in
some parts of the Eastern States of America,
the ceaseless cry is of the hunger and misery
and degradation produced by too much
population, but the evils born of too little
population are nowhere to be heard of. In
Australia, doubtless, the troubles that arise
from over-crowding will come in course of
time, but even if they are a wdiole billion
years ahead, that is no reason why they
should be hastened by so much as a single
day through the systematic importation of
Coolies, or Mongols, or Booth’s particular
brand of dull agriculturist. The indi-
viduals who preach the doctrine that what
Australia requires is more humanity, and
try to arouse enthusiasm thereby, might as
reasonably hold forth in a menagerie on the
subject of the lion’s great and immediate
need of buns—when they offer us men they
might as well hope to make a boom by
offering pig-iron to a cow ; the lion doesn’t
care for buns, and the cow has no craving
for pig-iron, and there the matter ends.
* * #
The oft-repeated tale about the great
resources that are lying idle for want of
hands is empty and unavailing. The
3,000,000 inhabitants of Australia are now
utilising all the resources which their num-
ber will permit ; if 3,000,000 more are
introduced they will probably utilise twice
as much soil, and grow twice as many vege-
tables, and as there will be twice as many
people to divide the proceeds, the situation
will be just the same. In any case the
resources will keep. The forests will not
bolt, nor will the soil clear out, and the
hidden gold will remain just where it is ; and
if these assets are not turned into money
this century they will be in the next, or, if
not then, they will come in useful a thousand
years hence, or if even that is too soon they
can wait ten thousand years, till the nation
is ready to employ them. And if the tropical
regions of Australia are unfit for the white
man’s occupation at the present time, it is
quite possible that some day or other in the
far-ofl future the earth will slew round on
its axis, and the Northern Territory will
rush unexpectedly into the temperate zone,
and Melbourne and Adelaide will shift down
into the land of ice and frost ; and then the
inhabitants of these cities will want the
empty regions around Port Darwin and the
Gulf as a place of refuge. Meanwhile, their
great resources are lying idle and neglected,
like the superfluous silverware on the rich
man’s sideboard, and if this fact is a reason
why the tropical provinces should be given
away to Chinamen and niggers, it is equally
a reason why Kin tore and such as he
should rush out on the instant and give their
plate to an African in order to get rid of it.
and why Booth should give his other pair of
boots—the one he isn’t wearing—to the first
mendicant he meets. This is,, no doubt,
selfish logic, but then everything on this
earth is selfish. And as there are no signs
of Kintore giving away his plate, or of
Booth handing over all his clothes savfe
those in immediate use, or of any other of
Australia’s critics bestowing all his super-
fluities on the poor, none of them have a
right to cast moral bricks on this account.
A Word to the Cabbage-Gardeners,
Nobody ever yet buried a dead superstition so
deep that it would never rise again. No one
ever yet hung a sufficiently heavy millstone to
the remains of a defunct lie, and sunk it so far
down in the sea of oblivion as to make sure that
it would never again come to the surface. In
the great economy of Nature nothing is ever
lost or destroyed—neither dust, nor worms, nor
drivel, nor brass gods, nor lies, nor continents,
nor old cranks, nor superstitions, nor any oilier
matter or substance whatever. Everything is
indestructible—it may alter its form and its
name, but the substance remains. The curse
that was uttered by some naked savage in the
year One is still floating intact somewhere in the
realms of space. The shadow of everything
that was ever done is photographed somewhere
on the atmosphere—the echo of everything that
was ever said or whispered is still existent and
in a good state of preservation. No ancient
error was ever lost or utterly extinguished ; it
may be buried to-day beneath a mountain of
convincing fact, but to-morrow it is sitting on
its old pedestal and waiting to be buried over
again. It is one long, incessant funeral, in
which the same old corpse is planted over and
over again, and as fast as it is interred it climbs
out of its sepulchre ready for another interment.
Therefore, the way of the reformer is particu-
larly hard, for when his great reformation is
finished the thing that has just been reformed
generally wants to be reformed ove” "gain
almost as badly as it did before.
# # *
The recent doings of the Victorian Legislative
Council are a striking proof of the indestructi-
bility of matter, and drivel, and superstition,
and all other tangible and intangible substances.
The subject under discussion was Premier
Munro’s One-man-one-vote Bill, and the con-
tented, old, fat men of the Upper House sat
down to consider the problem, and to decide
whether it was really true that all men were
intended to be free and equal. The question
was supposed to have been decided by divine
authority nineteen centuries ago. It had pro-
bably been settled on a dozen previous occasions
by a dozen great authorities, and every time the
world joyously accepted the doctrine of human
equality, and then went right on as before, and
worshipped the old god of Property just as if
there had been no reformation at all. A hun-
dred poets and philosophers had planked down
noble sentiments to the effect that money is in
itself an empty consideration, and that man, as
a being who ranks only a little below the
angels, is the same whether he is rich or penni-
less. From Solomon downwards the same
dogma has been repeated a thousand times, and
the ancient, bulky men of the Victorian Legisla-
ture have all a profound reverence for Solomon
and are ready to, swear by him as one unani-
mous fat Christian every time. Then they came
to exactly the same resolution that they would
have arrived at if there had been no Solomon, no
Jesus Christ, no Christianity, no reformation,
no progress—nothing. They decided that the
)■ ii who hadn't any property wasn’t worth a
( urre compared to the man who had property,
and they infixed the old plural-vote, and re-
erected the old, dead superstition that had been
buried so often, and which always came up
again as joyously as if it had never been buried
at all; and then went on their way rejoicing.
I’he old lies are imperishable. The believers
may diminish in number, but there will be
somebody left to prop up every ancient theory
to the end of time. Even Baal and Jupiter
probably have their worshippers still, if any-
body had time to hunt them up.
# # «
In re-constructing the ancient allotment-vote
the Council travelled on the same reasons which
have done duty since reasons of any kind were
first invented. The first speaker fairly repre-
sented the sentiments of all :
Mr. Dowling, a private member, moved the second
reading of the Constitution Amendment Bill. He sup-
ported the second reading so that amendments might be
made. This measure would take away privileges which
they had enjoyed for very many years.' Thrift, industry,
and temperance should be encouraged by a second vole.
This theory—which was promptly and un-
questioningly accepted—includes two assump-
tions. The first is that land or house pro-
perty—the only property which carries a double
vote —is always acquired by thrift, industry,
and temperance, and therefore by.giving an
extra vote to the landholder these desirable
traits will be encouraged in every case. The
second is that no other description of property is
ever acquired by the exercise of the three virtues
in question, and therefore no other description of
property should carry a special vote. The
landholder is to have one vote for his manhood
and another for his £4O allotment ; the house-
holder, on the other hand, will have one vote
for his manhood, but if he attempts to secure
another for his £GO piano, he will be badly left.
The allotment-vote is granted unless the
Council is guilty of hypocrisy and hard lying—
solely in recognition of the fact that allotments
are always gained by economy, hard work, and
temperance, and never by inheritance, by per-
sistent rum-selling, by illicit distillation, usury,
fraud, confidence tricks, dummyism, three-card
tricks, bookmaking, sweeps, forged title-deeds,
fan-tan, or any other baseness ; the piano-vote
is not granted, from which it may be reasonably
inferred that, in the opinion of the Victorian
Upper House, that instrument does not repre-
sent any virtue that ought to be encouraged,
and that the only way *o get a piano is by


extravagance, idleness, and persistent drunken-
ness. This is a strange and weird anomaly,
and if, as it pretends, the Council desires
to establish the plural - vote upon a really
moral basis, and to use it as a means of
promoting virtue, the advisability of granting a
separate vote for the piano should be at once
taken into consideration.
» * #
This, however, is only one side of a many-
sided problem. Clothes, like land, also repre-
sent industry and self-denial, and the necessity
for an additional vote for the well-clad elector’s
coat, hat and shirt is quite as urgent as the
necessity for a suffrage on account of some
cheap, neglected half-acre of mortgaged earth.
Under the present system, the individual who
goes in dirty rags, and puts the money
thus saved into land, is entitled to twice as
much political influence as the person who gives
up the latter indulgence, and avoids making
himself a common nuisance and an eyesore by
reason of his wearing apparel, and the injustice
cf this arrangement is sufficiently apparent.
The latter’s clothes and boots are the outward
evidences of his thrift and his temperance, and
if they are not allowed due weight at the poll,
then the whole political scheme is a lop-sided
and imbecile device, and the alleged motive
which prompted it is a lie and a base imposture.
The same rule applies to chairs, tables, carpets,
books and bric-a-brac, and it also holds good in
the matter of children. The individual in humble
circumstances whom Providence has burdened
with nine offspring is obviously entitled to a
property-vote on their account, inasmuch as,
but for them, he might reasonably have owned
sufficient property to entitle him to a vote, and
the want of that property arises from circum-
stances that are in a great measure beyond his
control. The fact that he has successfully
reared a large family is in itself an evidence of
more virility, industry, economy, and self-
denial than ten allotments with a vote attached
to each, and if the Victorian Council really
designs the plural suffrage as a recognition of
these virtues then the partial disfranchisement
of the man with a large family is ajr act of base
wickedness which cries aloud to Heaven for
redress. Further, the person whose extra vote
is founded on house-property, and who has the
misfortune to be burnt out so that his qualifica-
tion vanishes into thin air, should retain the
vote all the same ; for, according to the declara-
tion of the Victorian Upper House, his privilege
is granted, not through a mean desire to cringe
to any form of property, but as a recognition
of the virtues by which that property was
acquired. And though the property may have
vanished the virtues in question remain all the
same, and the State, by depriving the victim of
his political status under such circumstances, is
taking a base advantage of his misfortune, and
hitting an elector when he is down. Any State
that would do such a thing is too low and too
degraded for any white man to associate with.
* * *
One or two other aspects of the subject
are also worthy of attention. The neces-
sity of a vote for a life insurance policy
is too obvious to need explanation. A
life insurance policy for a good amount,
in a sound office, and regularly paid up,
represents the three • noble characteristics of
thrift, industry and temperance as effectually—
or more effectually than any allotment or
house property can do ; it also indicates fore-
sight, which is a virtue equally worthy of en-
couragement, and it is a sign of kindliness of
heart, inasmuch as the insurer evidently desires
not to leave those dependent upon him desti-
tute. The equity of granting a special vote-
under certain conditions—for broken legs, in-
flammation, domestic bereavement, and similar
afflictions is almost equally apparent. Ihe
workman who is disabled by a broken leg, and
yet gets through without outside assistance,
thereby manifests as much thrift and temper-
ance as would be evidenced by the possession of
a small patch of earth, for the patch of earth
merely proves that he had saved a certain
amount of money, and the broken leg—under
the circumstances above-mentioned —proves ex-
actly the same thing. Moreover, the damaged
limb is, in all probability, the only reason why
he doesn’t get the vote, that extremity having-
absorbed, in the shape of medical expenses, the
money which would otherwise have purchased
the necessary property. Therefore, if a soul-
less , Government refuses a property-vote to a
man with a broken leg—merely because he has
been unlucky—it thereby penalises him for
breaking his leg, which is as degrading and
unmanly as if it were to hang him for concuss-
ing his spine, or send him to penal servitude
for having rheumatism. Unless the \ ictorian
Upper House is a mere congregation of bowel-
less fat men without sympathy or human kind-
ness it will do something to remedy this awful
discrepancy right away.
* * *
The subject of plural voting, as a means of
encouraging temperance, economy, and indus-
try, is, however, an exceedingly large one, and
so far we have only touched on a few stray
illustrations of a very great matter. Fyen
granting, however, that the possession of a
certain bit of earth is, in itself, a solid evidence
of three pure and noble characteristics, and
assuming that no piece of land was ever acquired
by fraud or stealing, or by shunting an aged
parent into the workhouse, or by any other
degrading vice ; and further assuming that in-
dustry and the other two items are the only
things worth encouraging, and that charity,
chastity, piety, and matters like these are of
no account; and allowing, for the sake of argu-
ment, that the property-vote may not be quite
as often a reward for rum-selling or commercial
swindling as anything else, it is still obvious
that the Victorian Upper Chamber is only at
the beginning of its work. For it is evident to
anybody gifted with comprehension that the
three choice virtues which that body desires to
encourage cannot always break out in the shape
of land or rateable property, and by giving a
vote to land alone, and ignoring all other
evidences of genuine merit and nobility of soul,
the Council shows itself utterly indifferent to
all the claims of justice. And unless clothes
and pianos and broken legs and bedridden
relatives are similarly recognised at the polls
the plural voting scheme is, from first to last, a
degrading imposture and an outrage on every
principle of equity.
PLAIN ENGLISH.
An Incoherent Female.
The New South Wales Women’s Suffrage
League held a meeting last week, and Mrs. ,J.
B. Ashton seized the opportunity to make a
few incendiary remarks, in the course of which
she wandered far from the point, greatly dam-
aged the cause of womanhood suffrage with a
large section of the population, and made the
back-hair and the fringe of the League stand up
on end. The following is a summary of the
observations in question : —■
The simple words “ womanhood suffrage ” only fixed in
her mind more strongly two convictions that had boen
fairly well established there—(list, that abnormal intel-
lectual triumphs could never fully satisfy the feminine
nature ; and, second, that the suffrage once fairly attained
for women, their first work must be to amend the mar-
riage laws, or, blasphemous as the words might sound, do
away with them altogether. Marriage, as a solution of
the great sexual problem of to-day, was a failure—a
lamentable one—and to them, the small thinking section
in a world of marionettes, was given the righting
of a wrong that had endured for centuries. They
had grown to regard the marriage ceremony as
a Christian ordinance. It was purely a social cus-
tom. What was moral in one country’ stood for
nothing in another. Year after year sweet girls were
brought forward in the marriage market; year after year
the purest and best of them shrank back from the un-
equal contest between innocence and worldliness, ideality
and stern reality. No woman until she had known love
and achieved the dignity of motherhood was thoroughly
complete, and yet to oue-half the world of women, this
simple fulfilment of a natural destiny was denied. To
right this wrong, to set these matters upon a sound basis,
rested with the men and women of the future, when they
should have wrested from a conservative society the simple
privilege of working for the common good. And other
social problems, which women alone could help to solve,
awaited her aid and sympathy in the coming years.
It is the same old weary wail of the advanced
female over and over again. Marriage is a
failure— The Bulletin admits the fact freely.
But, then, nearly everything else that we know
of is a failure, too. Free-love is a failure ;
polygamy is a ghastly delusion; celibacy is a
fraud; and marriage, so far, is the smallest
failure of the lot—barring divorce. There
is one incurably weak point about the
advanced female ; she always wants to
abolish marriage, but she seldom has
the courage to definitely recommend any-
thing as a substitute. If free-love is to be
the substitute, then the average male will throw
out his partner by superior brute force as soon
as she becomes old and ugly, and leave her to
maintain the family, and the navigation of our
harbours and rivers will be hampered by the
remains of lost, deserted women who have
been ejected in their old age. Then, there is
further trouble, because women are too plenti-
ful, and a large proportion of the species are
unable to get married at all, and the “ dignity
of motherhood ” is denied them. This difficulty
may possibly be got over by polygamy, hut
then polygamy has been tried, and, so far, it
doesn’t amount to much ; or, again, it may be
surmounted by killing off all superfluous females
in infancy—only Mrs. Ashton doesn’t openly
recommend either alternative. Something
might be done by the establishment of
a. Socialistic system, so that all abandoned
children might be cared for by a State
department, and as all -property would belong
to the State, there would always be sufficient
funds on hand to meet this tremendous
liability, and then families could break up at
any hour of the day or night without hindrance;
but, again, Mrs. Ashton doesn’t seem to
say anything about Socialism. Another and
a shorter way would be to establish universal
celibacy, and let the human race, with all its
troubles and worries and problems, die out
altogether, but, if the modern advanced female
wants to die out, she never says so. She wants
to put things on a “ sound basis,” but she
never by any chance produces the basis.
Marriage, alleviated by divorce, has one recom-
mendation —it furnishes at least a partial
solution of the great difficulty about the
maintenance of the rising generation, ; ml fixes
the responsibility —to a certain extent at least-
on somebody. It does it badly, but then every
other scheme yet propounded seems to do it a
deal worse. It is a failure, but it is no good
exchanging it for a worse failure, and there is
nothing to be gained by abolishing it for the
sake of any incoherent female, unless she has
something to suggest that can take its place.
And, so far, no incoherent female that we know
of has suggested anything.
Prison Labour.
What to do with the imprisoned criminal is
one of the unsolved problems of civilisation.
The average criminal, in a very large propor-
tion of cases, is an individual who has no useful
calling—who knows no trade, and has no avail-
able means of livelihood. If he belongs to what
is commonly described as the “ lower ranks of
society he is a howling rowdy who appears in
the charge-sheet as a “labourer’ —in other
words, he is an individual who has no occupa-
tion, except—like the horse and the working
bullock —that of a common and precarious beast
of burden. If he is a little better dressed, and
has a little more knowledge of geography and
dead kings and similar subjects, he becomes a
money-lender’s jackal, or a iinance and com-
mission swindler, or an alleged broker with an
alleged office in a back street , or a hanger-on of
bogus companies ; and when lie gets into gaol for
some shady transaction concerning a bank, he
is probably entered as an “ agent ” —which also
means, in most cases, that he has no useful
occupation whatever. If he is simply shoved
into a whitewashed room, like a quantity of
coal in a cellar, or a wild animal in a zoological
garden, he is a useless expense to the com-
munity ; and when he comes out he must either
take to fraud or to plain, unvarnished burglary
as before. He never learned anything else, and
fraud or burglary, as the case may be, is the
only occupation he has to live upon. On the
other hand, if he is compelled to learn a trade,
and the products of his industry are sold in the
open market, he becomes a self-supporting in-
stitution during his term of imprisonment, and
when he is released the chances of his relapsing
into crime are diminished —in the case ot the
high-toned swindler very slightly, in that
of the common or garden malefactor —as has
been proved by actual experiment —at least
95 per cent. Man is a creature of habit.
The loafer who has led a precarious existence
by robbery with violence may be put through a,
course of seven years’ hard cabinet-making, and
when he is released the saw and the chisel have
become matters of custom ; he may have gone
through no process of repentance or moral
regeneration, yet he continues to live by
cabinet-making from sheer force of habit. But
if he is simply locked up in a cell, and haunted
by a parson, the cell and the parson also become
matters of habit, oidy as they do not constitute
a useful occupation he obviously cannot live by
them, and therefore his prison training is of no
use to him. Unluckily, however, that obtuse,
ynreasoning tiling known as constituted au-
thority always takes it for granted that
prison labour and cheap labour ought to
be synonymous terms. The prison labourer
becomes a species of confined blackleg, and
his work tends to reduce prices outside.
Sundry favoured firms which have gained
access to the capacious ear of those in power
buy up the products of gaol industry for a
song, and fetch it away surreptitiously by
night, and thus are enabled —while realising
large profits—to sell it at prices which are
absolutely ruinous for the workman who is not
blessed with free board and lodging in a peni-
tentiary. Then there arises an outburst of
frenzy against prison labour of all kinds, and a
demand is made that it should be entirely
abolished—that the criminal should be a dead
loss to the country during his term of confine-
ment, and should be turned loose the same
helpless and incapable individual that he was
at the beginning. This theory is entirely
unreasonable. It is a democratic maxim
that every man should work for his living
—if not voluntarily, then by compulsion.
It is also a Christian maxim that the sinner
should be reformed if possible, and the shortest
road to reformation is by placing him in a
position which makes honesty possible. Fur-
ther, it is better and cheaper that the garrotting
man or the burglar should be violently trans-
formed into a good tradesman, even if he
thereby increases the pressure of competition
upon other tradesmen, than that he should
come out to choke these same tradesmen on the
highway, and clean out their pockets as before.
The one evil lies in turning the criminal into a
cheap-labour machine. Every gaol ought to be
a huge workshop, run on pure business prin-
ciples. The products should be sold in the
large centres of population by auction, or
privately at ordinary trade rates. Then the
additional competition would be practically
unfelt, for the criminal population is not large
enough to seriously affect the labour market,
provided it works at the regular trade rates.
But so long as it is utilised on cheap Chinese
principles, a few hundred prisoners are suffi-
cient to depress the rate of wages throughout
a whole country.
The ancient bigots of Auckland (M.L.) have
founded yet another National Association. It
is built on the same plan as the Associations at
Sydney, Melbourne, and Brisbane; like them,
it is composed entirely of landlords, bankers,
merchants, brokers, squatters, commission-
agents, steamship-owners, and the like ; and it
lias the same hypocritical programme setting
forth that it wants to secure the due represen-
tation of all classes of society, and to prop up
the constitution, and look after the best interests
of everybody. Its first public act has been to
prepare a petition to Governor Onslow, de-
manding that he should make no further
appointments to the Legislative Council, and
as a scheme for recuring the duo repre-
sentation of all classes, and propping up the
Constitution, this is a really good beginning.
For during the last two years or more the
Maoriland Lower House has passed about eight
democratic measures, every one of which the
Council has either rejected, or else mutilated
so as to render it utterly abortive; therefore,
as the Lower House is distinctly representa-
tive of the people, and the non-representative
Chamber blocks every attempt it makes to
carry out the popular will, and as the Association
insists that no steps shall be taken to abolish
this deadlock, it follows that the Association
demands in effect that representative Govern-
ment shall be abolished. As matters stand at pre-
sent, the irresponsible nominees ask that things
should remain as they are, while the people’s
delegates want them to keep progressing, and
as the stagnant House has had its way for up-
wards of two years, the system of representative
government has practically been non-existent
for that period. This ancient stone-wall can
only be broken up by filling the Council with
fresh nominees, and the urgent request of the
alleged “ National ” party that no such move-
ment shall be permitted is a lurid commentary
upon its so-called principles. Moreover, there
is another aspect of the case which is equally
surprising. Governor Onslow is supposed
to act in all things by the advice of
his Ministers. The Ballance Cabinet—
which, by reason of its democratic ten-
dencies, is utterly loathed by the ancient Tory
party—undoubtedly contemplates a
batch of democrats to the Upper House, as
the only means of breaking up the existing
deadlock. Therefore, the Association requests
in the strongest and most direct language that
Onslow shall burst up the Constitution by
acting directly counter to his advisers in a
matter which belongs exclusively to their
.province. And yet it asserts that its object is
above all things to preserve the Constitution,
and keep it intact. In other words, it wants
to break the thing, and then preserve it after
it is broken.
Sydney D.T.’ again touts undisguisedly for
the creation of local electric-lighting mono-
polies. Some of its arguments are curiously
wide of the subject of attack, which is in
reality the City Council’s proposal to undertake
the work municipally; others are simply
inverted in the interest of dividend-pay-
ing concerns which live on the entrails
of populous communities. Briefly, the D.T.
argues that, because the Sydney Council’s
present scheme of electric-lighting is ” tardily
conceived and miserably inefficient,” therefore,
it should not be permitted to carry out any
scheme no matter how ably conceived or practi-
cally efficient it may be. Because the Council
proposes to experiment with the high-tension arc
system, which is, rightly or wrongly, saidby some
(interested but possibly truthful) experts to be
dangerous, therefore, it is to be debarred alto-
gether from undertaking any other system of
electric-lighting. TheD.T. declares the Council
to be per.se incapable of dealing with the subject.
Why a corporate body of men not interested in
paying themselves dividends should be incap-
able while dividend makers are capable, is not
stated. It is to be presumed that the Council,
which will not need to run a city-lighting
project as a money - piling concern, will
be able to pay a competent electrical
staff as good salaries as a private syndi-
cate that demands profits of from 10 per
cent, upwards to —the limit of public forbear-
ance. The D.T. also girds at the idea that the
Council, after street-lighting, should seek to
make money out of private companies by
issuing leases conferring on them the right to
illuminate private establishments. To ordinary
intelligences, it would seem more sensible for
the Council — i.e., the ratepayers—-to make
money out ,of syndicates than to empower
syndicates to make money out of the ratepayers.
But the D.T. is aghast at the bare thought of
the money of the ratepayers being “ expended
in works which private enterprise could carry
out as well or better.” Pish ! If the project
is good enough for private enterprise, it must,
by the nature of the conditions, be better still for
the combined enterprise of the ratepayers. The
alarm sought to 100 created, concerning the
“perils” to which “the City Corporation, in
their new zeal as amateur electricians, propose
subjecting the citizens of Sydney,” may be
laughed to death. In all measures of this kind,
Parliament can adopt a clear course in the
public interest—(l) grant no special privileges
to any private syndicate so long as a local cor-
poration is willing to undertake the task ; (2)
insist upon provisions ensuring public safety in
any Bill, whether that Bill relates to a syndi-
cate or to a municipal corporation. The public
squelched the tram way-grab (inaugurated by one
or more of the D.T. directors); let it now squelch
the electric-lighting grab now in course of in-
cubation.
♦
Matthias Larkin, a gentleman “prominent
in religious circles in South Melbourne,” and a
godly auctioneer in a large way of business, was
last week compelled by force of circumstances
to admit that he had plundered a building-
society of some ten or fifteen thousand pounds.
Matthias was secretary to several highly re-
spectable institutions, the building society
amongst them, and this being the concern that
offered the most opportunities for a wholesale
loot, he had “played up” the savings of his
keeind friends and brethren until his brain be-
came unequal to any further manipulation of
the books, whereupon he bowed'his head in
shame and hoped the Lord would forgive him.
Living in a well-feathered nest, and with his
bank-balance increasing every day, Matthias
wasn’t satisfied. The average poor clerk,
who tries to make enough money to fur-
nish a four-roomed cottage by backing
horses, and drops his master’s petty cash
over the wrong Malvolio, would, in five
cases out of six, have been a good, upright
fellow for life if someone had given him £2OO
to get married on. Avery small amount of
capital would suffice to make most first offenders
as honest as the Judge who gives them six
months, and the shopman, with a wife and
family, who pilfers to make both ends meet on
355. a week is almost invariably brought back
to the path of rectitude by a “ rise ” of ten
shillings. There was no sort of excuse for
Matthias to steal. In the days of his pros-
perity he hankered after fabulous wealth, and
though he used to wag his head and sigli
sympathetically over sermons on humility, or
discourses on the vanity of piling up riches
in this world, he was all the while
concocting schemes for buying land to
an amount far beyond his ample means.
And when the land-boom burst this representa-
tive pietist hadn’t sufficient manhood to go
bung. He embezzled and went on praying
more vigorously than ever. Such is the in-
fluence of “ position,” however, on the grovelling
soul of a daily newspaper that the Age was
unable to conceive the possibility of Matthias
Larkin getting his deserts like the vulgar
species of malefactor. In an apologetic tone
that alleged organ of the masses referred to the
shortcomings in Larkin’s cash, and added,
“ the idea appears to be held that gentlemen
who entertained a sincere regard for Mr.
Larkin would not now leave him entirely to his
own devices and would manifest the feeling by
making up the amount of his discrepancies. ’
Which is a distinct suggestion that the big
embezzler deserves to be saved from gaol or
have his sentence reduced 90 per cent, by in-
fluential friends, notwithstanding, that a poor
devil who prigs a pound of butter can’t get his
felony compounded so long as poverty remains
the worst of all crimes in the social calendar.
A broken-down wretch named Fownes
died the other day in New York. A few years
ago he was a doctor with a fashionable practice
and a pile of money, but in an evil hour he
became a slave to cotfee, and went to the bad. He
spent a large fortune in constructing new coffee-
pots and improved coffee-roasters; and his practice
broke up, because he left his patients to die while
he was grinding coffee-beans on some entirely
fresh principle, or fooling with some device to
bring out the aroma. Then he got into debt, and
his furniture was seized, and he was ejected from
his mansion ; and at last he became a poor, miser-
able, shattered coffee-fiend, and died in a cellar,
prematurely old, and quite alone. His only assets
were the clothes he wore and 24 coffee-pots. Any
young man who rashly contemplates abandoning
whisky and taking to the flowery paths of the old,
saddle-coloured beverage, should be warned by his
awful fate, for the coffee-bean goeth before a fall,
and he who warmeth the coffee-pot in his bosom
shall be stung thereby. Also the chicory herb is a
mocker which rushes down a steep place into the
sea; and the tea-plant goeth about like a roaring
shrub seeking whom it may devour; and the
cocoa-tree cometh like a thief in the night, any
raiseth Cain generally.

My Dear Moorabinba, —
Sydney Women Suffragists are publishing loud, in-
dignant cries in the daily press at the advocacy of
hymeneal Freetrade put forth at a recent hen-convention
by a married member, who stood up and railed at the holy
state of matrimony saying (a la Mona Caird) that the
league “should amend the marriage laws or doaway with
them altogether.” Which was the signal to fall to.
There isn’t enough matrimony to go round, according to
the advanced lady in question, and lots of women shed
tears over their “incompleteness’’and unutterable failure
because they have never been called upon to minister to
the whims of a lord and master. Now, so far as 1 can
ascertain, this yearning after matrimony, pur et simple ,
isn’t so very general, after all. Every girl dreams of a
rich husband, but very few envy the hum-drum connubial
joys of the worn-visaged matron acting as general
servant, nurse, companion, and valet to a man whose
commercial value is attested by the smallness of his
salary. Tor it is a utilitarian age ; love in a bark-hut is
scratched in the field of starters for the Hymeneal Plate,
and many a woman writes “Miss” on her visiting-cards
because Gussie laid a wealth of undying worship but
only four pounds a week at her feet. There is altogether
too much sympathy 7 wasted on the unmarried woman.
She is generally a cold, callous, speculative darling who
doesn’t melt into the first arms that open to receive her,
and who dies under a willow, simply because the only man
she e\ er lo\ ed had small wages, and her price was four
figures.
Buccaneer Stanley, now being towed round by the only
Smythe, had a decent reception on his first appearance
in Australia, at Melbourne Opera House on 11th inst.
There was a crowded house, headed by Gov. Hopetoun,
accompanied by Hon. Violet Willoughby in a silver-grey
gow n with a big crimson rose in the front. I went to
reverence and remained to quiz. The hero of the hour
turned out to be a medium-sized, grey-headed brigand in
a dress-suit that didn't fit him, especially in the bagleto,
and who talked English rather stiffly—like a man whose
tongue has been somewhat cricked by words like “mat-
■ okotloto” and other double-jointed African remarks,
md who rubbed his hands like a shop-walker as he talked
in a worn-out, deprecatory sort of voice through a lecture
.“How I Found Livingstone”) which would go better, to
my way of thinking, were a little more naked savage and
war-whoop thrown in, and a lot of bathos, and_Bib!e, and
British cant left out. Mr. S. produced a rather bad im-
pression when, in reply to a scratch-back address from
the Victorian Geographical Society, who begged to be
allowed to call him a member, he arose and ungracefully
said; “that though he had been elected member of all
the leading European geographical societies, he would
have much pleasure,” &c. And yet the V.G.S. cheered
him.
The attention of the house was drawn just as much to
the box where Mrs. Stanley and her mother sat, as it was
to the little man in front of the large map on the stage.
Mrs. S. is a straight-featured lady, who wears her dark
hair in sausage-rolls down the back of her head, and has
an English cheek as red as a rose. She wore a white
gown on her Australian debut, and all through the talkee-
talkee (she must know it all off backways and sideways

with raven hair, who wore a cream lace scarf a la Spanish
mantilla, and appeared to doze gently most of the time.
At the end of the lecture, Mr. S. and party were intro-
duced to the Governor, who remained seated in his box
while Master Wallington, in scarlet facings, went behind
and rounded the explorer up.
Cardinal Moran’s strictures on the “ Protestant nuns ”
to be established in Australia by the Anglican churches
are very true, after all. Protestant nuns nearly all slide
over to the Pope, or bolt back to the world, the flesh, and
the Bogie Man. “The religious life,” said the Cardinal,
“must be real to be sustained. There were two kinds of
soldiers—the real and the theatrical,” &c. quite so, your
reverence. Protestant “sisters” are never very real.
They can marry if they get the chance, and, this being
so, there will always be the greatest number of nuns
around the celibate incumbent and the curate with the
high forehead, and when a whole sisterhood admires one
inoffensive sky-pilot it isn’t likely that there will be any-
thing but jealousy and wool-work shoes come out of that
nunnery. By the way, Protestant nuns are an old story
at that ritualistic fane, St. David’s, Hobart. There they
wear long black robes and are usually discovered dusting
the ornaments on the altar when the devout drop in for
afternoon prayer. They aren’t very communicative to
the enquiring stranger, but they give a dim, religious
aspect to the premises generally, and three of them flut-
tering in a dark corner make the worlding’s flesh creep in
a manner that is probably good for his soul.
The latest Sydney divorce was brought about in rather
an unusual manner. Instead of the guilty wife falling
stiffly on to her deceived husband’s collar and weeping
her comforting statements all down the back of his neck
10, and behold, the gay Lothario dropped in casually one
evening, and knelt at the feet of the plaintiff, and gave
himself and the lady away with a minuteness of detail
that made hubby reach for his horse-whip. But Lothario
so wept and implored his enraged victim not to “put a
head on him,” summarily, that the latter thought better
of it, and is going to take it out in damages. But what a
foolish effect—Petrarch conscience-stricken at the feet of
“Mr, Laura!”
A well-known Melbourne sporting man wore a woman’s
gold cable bangle on Cup-day—obviously the property of
some fair syren who was indifferent to the state her
jewellery was kept in, for it had the appearance of a
bracelet accustomed to a hard life—the banged-about ex-
pression of a wristlet which is pulled over a semi-soiled
kid glove one moment and dropped among beer-dregs and
cigar-ash the next, and it somehow seemed to represent the
gentleman who had borrowed it for a Mascotte in a shift-
ing light. You could almost see the lady handing it ever
to him in curl papers and pink dressing-gown.

The wedding of Miss Ada Mason (for ten years governess
to Lady Jersey’s children) to Professor Allen, of the Mel-
bourne University, took place at the Anglican Church,
Sutton forest, on tlth inst. Lord Jersey gave the bride
away, and the Jersey children acted as bridesmaids and
page—the girls wearing pale-blue silk frocks and posies
of yellow roses, presented by the bridegroom. The bride
woie white silk trimmed with chiffon and lilies of the
' ft hey, and wore a diamond brooch (Lord Jersey’s gift)
and a diamond bracelet from the bridegroom. At break-
fast, laid on the verandah, Lady Jersey proposed the
health of the bride and bridegroom, who commenced their
honey moon at Bowral. Lord and Lady Jersey presented
the bride with a diamond brooch, a diamond and pearl
biooch, an inlaid cabinet, and a cheque.
Lots of girls involve themselves in that boisterous after-
dance, “Kitchen Lancers,” with a certain amount of feat
and trembling, for great are all the croppers therein if
you lose grip and get giddy, and there is a possibility of a
short, sharp snapping of brittle bones. During Cup week
the Block was paraded by a pretty blonde with her arm
in a sling who knows all about it. “ I was darncing the
kitchen larncers, you know.”
An American poetess says that all bachelors over 40
should be taxed according to the number of single women
o' el ' the proceeds to be used for the support of maiden
ladies. But this would be simply jumping on their own
orange-blossom, as it were, if the single women t°ok it
up. No woman would advertise herself as an advanced
' irgin to get some of the bachelor’s cash by means of a
hag-tax. No, she would rather put more cotton-wool in
her jersey and a youthful touch of perexide on her scanty
back hair, call herself 28 to the census-fiend, and trust to
Cupid to pull her through.

The P. of W. and his charmingly frescoed help-mate
must present rather a dazzling appearance, unless you go
prepared to adore them through a piece of burnt glass ;
not even the most fashionable couple at Flemington on
Derby day looked a quarter as brilliant as Albert E. and
his consort are said to have looked at Cowes on a recent
occasion. She wore a costume of white serge braided in
white, the long pea-jacket lined with scarlet silk, a white
yachting cap and yellow boots, and H.R.H. himself was
gorgeous in a chocolate brown suit, tan shoes, and a pink
shirt, cuffs and collar! He also probably' ran to a crimson
pocket-handkerchief, and a primrose boutonniere mea-
suring twelve inches across, and when the breeze agitated
the atmosphere, it’s ten to one that the Royal chromo-
trope exhibited a pair of red-silk socks.
I had a chat with a Melbourne lady-doctor the other
day—don’t you want to hear all about it ? You could
trace the woman even in the waiting-room, with its little
gipsy tables and their fancy-work covers—a piano in one
corner, with a text above it, “He that believeth in me
hath everlasting life’’—put up, perhaps, as a delicate
insinuation as to the powers of the doctress. I went
early, but the throng of afflicted was so large that I
endured a dreary wait ere I could be given ten minutes
tite a tete—so you see that woman-doctors are not going
to starve yet awhile. I found my quarry seated in a
surgery, got up like a boudoir with fans and fancy-work—
as different as possible from the depressing leather fur-
niture and calf-bound books of the average medical man’s
lair. She was a tall, good-looking woman, with dark hair
and piercing grey eyes, with a masculine strength of
expression in them, and she was neatly gowned in dark-
blue braided serge. Eighteen months’ practice in Mel-
bourne has been rewarded by flocks of patients, mostly
women, altho’ several men (most of ’em parsons) have
come to have their pulses felt by her slim, white fingers.
She has been invited to consult with leading medical men
who have given her every encouragement. One brought
his daughter to her for treatment; could confidence
farther go? and, in short, she finds the practice of her
profession a pecuniary success.
A certain musical lady, whenever she gets an engage-
ment among new people, always attends with herpockets
bulging with photographs of “dear English scenes—the
home of my youth,” &c. But, as ill-luck would have it,
she showed her bale of relics to a snag fresh from ’Ome
the other day. When she proudly produced a full-sized
cabinet of an important-looking building which she was
about to proclaim as her childhood’s happy home, the im-
portation blurted out “ Why, that’s the northern aspect
of Wycombe Abbey—your Lord C’arington’s place, don’t
you know !” Those photographs have had a rest ever
since.
Yours affectionately,
SAPPHO SMITH.
Taranganba again !
It is most convenient to have a first-class
room near tram stopping places, where ladies
can rest themselves, and if they choose, have a
good cup of tea, or other refreshments. We
understand the Country Milk Go’s. “Cascade”
80 & 82 Elizabeth-street, near King-street, is just
the place; it is certainly the largest and best
conducted room in Sydney, and is getting quite
the fashionable resort for afternoon tea. We also
understand that ladies who so desire can have
parcels sent there so that they can call for them
just before taking the tram for home.*
POLO.
He was a brave young- polo knight,
And close to saddle stuck,
What time the steed jumped left and right
And eke would try to buck ; -
But when the rearing nag became
The stock of startled wonder,
He stood his feet on either side,
And the pony walked from under.

S.M. Herald, 10th Nov. :
The fiftieth anniversary of the birth of the Prince of
Wales was celebrated, as it usually is in New South
Wales, with the utmost enthusiasm.
That scribe hasn’t recovered from the last cele-
bration of the fiftieth anniversary.
Maurice Dalton, aged 73 years, was hanged
at Darlinghurst prison on Tuesday morning for
the murder of his wife who had, from the evidence
given at the trial, “ nagged ” him into frenzy. Of
course nagging is no valid excuse for murder, but
under the circumstances, it might have been
thought that the State could easily have afforded
to let a miserable old wretch live out the remainder
of his days in gaol—that it could without any
mock sentiment or bogus respect for grey haired
savagery have extended to the septuagenarian
homicide the consideration which certain tribes
of Queensland niggers extend to white horses—
“ Ole fellow yarraman.” But the Dibbs Govern-
ment isn’t built that way. It believes in the
gallows, alike for boys of 17, and men of 77. As
a consequence (we quote from the Australian
Star, the Dibbs Government’s special organ):—
The spectacle was a horrible one as Dalton’s lifeless
body turned slowly, and the blood poured from the
gaping wound in the throat and ran in a bright red
stream, warm and rich with life, down his legs and rippled
into the pit below.
You can always bet that anything to which the
Sydney evening papers de'vote three columns
apiece, has a strongly civilising tendency.
Snatched from the grave. Mrs. M.
O'Connell writes from Windsor, Oct. 3, 1801:
‘‘ For many years I have been a victim to palpita-
tion of the heart and extreme nervousness, and
was induced by a friend to try Marshalls’ Tonic
Nervine, when to my joyful surprise a complete
change took place. I became once more sound
and well, my whole system becoming renovated,
and appetite and digestion good, therefore I can-
not help adding my testimony to the wonderful
qualities of your wonderful medicine. I know it
matched me from the grave.”
Champions in arena fistic,
Crowned in circles gladiatorial,
Matched were they for countless sovereigns,
They to fight unto a finish,
They to tap the ruddy claret,
Dreaded “ knock-out’ - also risking
In each other’s gore to wallow.

Curiosity to see a man so notorious as
11. M. Stanley, combined with the sanguine hope
that he would prove an entertaining narrator of
his doubtful doings on the Dark Continent
attracted four large, serious-looking audiences to
Melbourne Opera House last week. A few of the
people present on each of these sorrowful occa-
sions were sincere believers in Stanley and his
great work. ’ Others had come prepared to be
persuaded that he was something better than a
mere filibuster, while to the great majority he was
little more than a name that had been cropping
up in newspapers, cablegram, and paragraphs
for a long series of years. The one established
tact about Stanley was that he had “found
Livingstone,” the further fact, that his so-called
discovery was a small achievement compared with
Livingstone’s quiet way of getting there in the
hrst instance, not being generally known. It was
sufficient that one of the Best advertised men in the
world had undertaken to walk upon the stage and
deliver a lecture upon himself. Whether Stanley’s
admirers got their money’s worth out of the hero
is less certain than that the people who went to
be persuaded came away as wise as when thev
entered the theatre. Concerning Stanley’s pre-
tentions to entertain the general public with a
description (apocryphal or otherwise) of his
d lu nt;U u es ™ ere can scarcely be two oninions, or
it there be, the second opinion is confined to the
writers of notices for daily newspapers. A publi-
cation like the Age may choose to represent the
ex-agent for the New York Heealu as a first-
class lecturer a man who has an absorbingly in-
teresting story to tell, and tells it with impressive
effect. Unprofessional critics and unprejudiced
listeners, however, will find Stanley the dreariest
or bores, reading, in measured, monotonouspulpifc
tones, a sermon that contains little to fix one’s
attention and much that explains why sassiety
after a few dinner-party experiences of the
explorer, ceased to lionise him. One of his dis-
courses deals mainly with latitudes, longitudes
square miles, and the number of cubic feet of
water discharged into the sea by the Congo, which
is a broad-fast flow-ing-river-of-a-rich-brown
co-lour. rhe awful absence of “ go,” humour, or
anything like real pathos in Stanley’s lectures
(which are mercifully brief) is as noticeable as the
obtrusive pains he takes to adopt a subdued man-
ner of sounding his own trumpet. His references
to royalty, as when he solemnly descants upon the
noble munificence of King Leopold,” betray the
size of his sun-dried soul, and his self-congratula-
tion over the work accomplished in the Congo
region, where 3672 police and 37 military
stations (with power to add to their number) are
the outcome of Stanley’s visits, and the result of
his purchasing a large chunk of continent for two
pans of stockings and a bootjack stamp him as a
born missionary of the deepest dye.
* * *
Hie present stage appearance of this un-
questionably fearless and tough adventurer is a
severe disillusion. In evening-dress the little
hero seems almost podgy, probably owing to the
amount of flannel swathings he must necessarily
wear for protection against “jungle cramps,”and
Ins tat face expresses nothing in particular. But
for the white curly hair on his large bullet head
it would be difficult to recognise, from his por-
traits the man who “rescued ” Emin Pasha from
the danger that he immediately returned into,
and who now gives himself clean away by his
laboured attempts to belittle Emin and cover up
the gory scandals for which the last Stanley ex-
pedition is well, celebrated. Pictorially, the
lecturer is as disappointing as his lectures. He
ought to come on in a Darkest Africa get-up.
carrying implements of destruction in his hands
instead of seeming like old Ned Holloway at a
small tea-party. He also requires a few dissolving
views, likewise a brass band, and an incidental
ballet, and if he could manage to throw
wt ‘T°r Adams won the Battle
of Waterloo by way of a peroration, his
audiences might go home happier than they
look under present conditions. Just now Stanley
is doing Geelong, where his clerical delivery will
touch m deeply, but on Saturday he is said to be
due at Melbourne again—in the Town Hall. The
wily Smythe has this distinguished missionary at
the end of his little string.

Poor old “ Rob Roy,” a drama which was
unfortunately born before comic operas were in-
vented, enjoyed a gloomy resurrection at Mel-
bourne Royal on Saturday. The Braw Scots
rolled up in force to the spectacle and bore with
it patiently enough, but thev neglected to demon-
strate over passages intended to be stirring, and
displayed no national indignation when some
Sassenach infidels in the gallery began to poke
borak at Macgregor’s outlaw followers. De-
scribed on the playbill as “Sir Walter Scott’s
immortal work,” this stage-version of “Rob Roy”
consists of selections from Scott, interspersed
with silly fustian speeches and musical items.
Good singing and a lavish display of sturdy
supers might, even in this irreverent age, carry
the antiquated production to a successful issue,
but, as matters were on Saturday, there seems
little hope for the Highland chieftain. Walter
Bentley’s powers as an actor are not to be fairly
judged by his first satisfactory appearance in a kilt

There are some very flue airs in “The
Corsair,” particularly in the Byronical privateer’s
chestnut wig donned for the occasion by Fanny
Liddiard. Anyhow, the music, particularly com-
poser Rice’s very own, is lively, and his musical
curry is served up at Sydney Royal after the
manner of “Evangeline,” with much gorgeous
drapery, scenic effect, and spectacular display.
Its legs are as limber and its silks as sheeny as
the veriest voluptuary could desire, and themany-
ourtair.e 1 harem, the lugger, winged “ with
scented sails of silver silk, whereon the great, red
gladioli burn,” are gaudy as the visions of a Per-
sian poet after a jamboree on sherbet cooled with
snowballs. The music here and there is a little
out of Fanny Liddiard’s reach, but she reaches it
somehow without straining her tendon Achilles,
and looks as plump and tight-fitting as though
slic’d never known the influenza. “ The Spanish
Duet,” sung by Conrad and ids captive, Medora,
played by Virginia Earl, is one of the best
vocal items in the show, but the singing of the
“elite quartette,” Messrs. Edwards, Fitzgerald,
Ure and Brown, hit our tympanum most agreeably.
The gasconading, gore-quaffing Birbanto is
played by George Walton. George’s singing is
not calculated to gain him a golden harp and
halo, but, even with Storey’s stepping in
mind, his,dancing is a fling of beauty and a jig
forever. Fat Fortesene’s humour is to be guaged
chiefly by dropping a penny in the slot, and the
spectacle of a twenty-stunner in ballet skirts is
not a thing of kaleidoscopic variety. The rest of
the fun is contributed by Harry Leston, as
Yussuf, a green-kilted Irish dealer in femininity,
who dances well and sings decently. Large house
on first night ; business, so far, fair.
ss * $
“Eolith,” revived at Sydney Her Majesty's,
is as good a thing as George Rignold has ever
done in the melo-dramatio line. This light of
other days seems likely to revive the lately-
tarnished glories of the Pitt-street theatre.
Having reduced his weight by aid of the influenza,
Rignold is now sufficiently attenuated to look
somewhat like Frank Darlington, but his perform-
ance is scarcely up to the standard of that he
showed us some ten years ago. It’s a pity the
prevailing epidemic wasn’t also laid on to the long
waits of first night, for the show nearly over-
lapped the rim of Sunday morning. William
Rignold, who plays the vicar, looks much
like an enlarged Bishop Barry, and other-
wise fills the clerical gaiters admirably.
Harry Jewett does his best to make the villain
unconventional and original. Larry is right into
the hands of Sweeney, and Jem Hazlitt, Joe
Tolano, and Stirling Whyte, are good as gold in
small parts. The histrionic event of the show,
however-, is the Sydney debut of Rose Conroy, a
young lady who dropped on these shores without
any flourish of trumpets, and who has previously
appeared at Melbourne Princess. As Eve de
Malvoisie the stranger is bright and picturesque;
apparently a finished actress, she is likely to
become a favourite. In the unavoidable absence
of Mrs. Rignold, Roland Watts-Phillips plays
Willie Spratley very well indeed, but we much
prefer the lady in skirts. Kate Bishop is well
placed as Mrs, Walsingham. If “ Youth ” doesn’t
prove a big go it will be only because there is no
money left in Sydney.
IK IK IK *
Eating is a purely animalish occupation
at the best of times; mimicry is a monkey-like
faculty that is most apeish when its votaries pre-
tend to eat something. We are led to these
reflections by beholding Percy Lyndal and
George Titheradge affecting to munch bill-
stickers’ paste and green playbills in “School” by
way of chicken and salad, and pretending to
enjoy their meal. Seeing “School ” again one is
more than ever convinced that it is a decidedly
clever piece of fooling. The author knew his
public, albeit a bit of a snob and a worshipper of
the golden calf. For instance, Beaufoy, a lordling
just intelligent enough to keep flies off the
ocular that isn’t eye-glassed, pokes Krux
with his rifle to attract his attention, and
Poyntz, a money ; hunting loafer, thrashes
the down-trodden usher, because he answers truth-
fully the hysterical queries of a doddering old
dude. “School” is doing good biz. for a third
revival, but will take unto itself an affix on Satur-
day when “Schoolmistress ” goes up with Maggie
Moore, after an absence of eight months, in her
old part. Manager Magee has triumphed over
influenza and lung-inflammation and is now to be
seen in front every evening.

It is altogether improbable that Jennie
Lee will ever get anything to suit her so well as
“Jo.” Still, as Collie in “ Run Wild ”at Sydney
Garrick she has better stuff to handle than has
recently fallen to her lot. Moreover, all round,
the little comedy-drama is well handled. Business
has been fairly good, but the last nights are
announced, and “Jo,” the everlasting, probably
goes up on Saturday.

With the fine weather Bondi has been so
overcrowded that Wyburd is thinking of wading
in to reclaim the Pacific. The sea-caves are cool
in the hottest days, and the ozone to be obtained
there can lie guaranteed of the best quality. Also
the glass for the new tanks has arrived from
England—everything in N.S. Wales arrives more
or less from England—and the cool, refreshing
fish will shortly be back in his grotto.
