Wiegand’s afternoon organ recitals don’t,
as a rule, draw more than 500 people to Sydney's
Town Hall, and to an outsider it looks as if the
.time had come to let the people of Sydney hear
their own pipe from the ground floor for a modest
threepence. Looking round at the rapt faces of
most people when the organist is dealing with the
solemn and grand, one finds it easy to account for
the goody-goody feeling that used to usher him
out of church in his callow days. ’Twas the
voluntary did it.
IK iK IK *
The Gerald-Duff season at Adelaide Royal
has been so far a financial failure. “Human
Nature” is a dreary, miserable, inane sort of
attempt at dramatic plot, and being acted for the
most part in a feeble, dreary style by a company
not overburdened with talent, has not caused
much of a stir. Following this up, the production
of Gerald and Duff’s “ original ” version of
“ Barnes of New York ’’ did not improve matters.
Possibly it was too original. The productions
have been well staged, but the contract was too
large for the material at hand, although Gerald,
Geo. Leopold and Alice Norton all worked hard
to make things go, and to bear up beneath a load
of mediocrity. “The Pointsman” will work his
feelings off next. Snazelle, opening to wretched
houses, has worked up a handsome business at the
Town Hall. Miss Snazelle and Philip Langdale
contribute towards the show.
IK & * iK
That floating relic, the ancient convict-
hulk “ Success,” is moored this week at Circula l '
Quay, Sydney. It is a massive affair with a
leprous exterior, ponderous, exceedingly aged*
pimpled, and yellow, and crusted over with old-
time villainy. The outside is solid, and the decks
are solid, and down in the dim interior there are
rows upon rows of cells, where wax images of the
forgotten convicts loaf amid the gloom, and the
shadows, and the old, cast-off smells, like dead
men damned in a tank. They are a clammy
race, these deceased wax malefactors, and very
cold and fish-like and unresponsive, and they look
as if they had been waiting for centuries in a
grotto, and nothing had ever came of all their
waiting. Some of them look ferocious, but the
majority are only numb and depressed; it is
difficult to construct a howling wax fiend in any
case, and, besides, a man who roosts in solid dark-
ness, with nothing but the heave of his murky
tomb for company, gives up looking ferocious
after a while and looks mean and miserable
instead. Now and then the System took them
out, and hitched them to iron necklaces between-
decks, by way of change and variety; and at
other times it hauled them up on deck by a wind-
lass, and shoved them, five or six at a time,
into a cage, and washed them with a hose,
while a warder roused them up with a long
broom. It was a remarkably clean thing, the
old System, and it would take a prisoner’s skin
off if it couldn’t get the dirt away by any other
means. The murder of Captain Price is thrown
in as a joyous tableau, witli the officer on the
floor, and a wax homicide demolishing him with a
pickaxe; and one end of the lower deck is fitted
up as a sylvan glade, with the Kelly Gang boiling
its billy among the ferns. Ned Kelly's armour is
also on exhibition —a huge mass of rusty iron
that is calculated to be a good load for a camel,
and there are a band, and electric - lights, and
other attractions. The “ Success ”is doing well
in Sydney, but the enterprising management
propose shortly navigating the show to England,
which seeems a decidedly reckless undertaking.
For somewhere out on the waste of waters her
cargo of painted goblins may come to life again,
and seize the vessel, and cast the crew overboard
among the foam and the dolphins, and then they
will sail away, all yelling and cursing and clank-
ing their chains, and become a spectral crew
working out an interminable sentence on the sea.
And then the ghosts will rush shrieking up and
down the old teak decks, and wash each other
with the spectral pump in the bows, and haul
each other up from below by the time-worn wind-
less, and there will be howls and groans and blue
fire, and horrors unutterable.

Sells’ great circus has been struck by an
undeserved calamity at Sydney. On account of
the fact that two of its horses—since destroyed
had developed glanders, the entire stock of trained
horses—some 30 or more, comprising the finest
collection of the sort ever seen in Australia—has
been put into quarantine. However, everything
is expected to be right for the opening, on
this Thursday night, 19th November.
At Poverty Point.
Elton talks of framing a kind, patronising letter from
a parson's wife, who, having met the renowned William,
some years ago, would like him to come and give a choice
assortment of “character sketches” at her little cake
fairs. The cordial tone of the epistle suggests that
lemonade would be supplied gratis to the artist between
his sketches. Billy feels aggrieved all the same. What
has he ever done that a parson’s wife should take him to
a fair cake?

Alfred Dampier relates how he and Henry Irving were
once in the same company, in his early career. It became
necessary for the management to “weed out” a few.
Irving was sent adrift and Dampier remained.
On Saturday night, during the performance of
“Youth,’ at Sydney Her Majesty’s, the transport, laden
with chicken-breasted warriors, was gaily leaving the
wharf although her stern-line was still fast. Whereupon
somebody in the gallery, in a voice like unto Captain
Carey, of the Monowai, shouted out, “ let go yer afther-
shpring there,” and the house appreciated that one bit of
relief to an otherwise monotonous proceeding.
Nellie Farren told somebody or other before she left
Australia that the Gaiety “ladies” don’t appear in the
streets with painted faces. Now, it she had told us that
none of them ever got hosed off the deck of a mail-
steamer at three a.m. for generally painting the boat red
we might have believed her.
Sydney D.T. in a notice of “The Corsair,” now running
at the Royal, refers to Geo. K. Fortescue as “a rich
humorist.” Why not “.fat,” and what’s the matter with
“ greasy ? ” The same journal also refers to that adipose
infliction as being “ delightfully funny.” So he is—just
about as gracefully humorous as a side of beef, as
delicately mirth-provoking as a string of German sausages,
or as daintily amusing as a beer-vat in tights.
On convict hulk Success. Showman (pointing to some
firearms): “Those guns, too, are 100 years old.” By-
stander : “ But they have percussion-locks.” Showman :
“ 1 can’t help that, they belonged to the ship when she
was fitted out in 1790.” Bystander (rubbing lock with
finger); “But they’re branded “Tower, 1835.” Exit
showman.

The once famous Trehelli Bettini, best woman-boy
that ever wore tights in Italian Opera, has been struck
by paralysis down one entire side of her bedy. At a
Copenhagen concert recently she was led on to the plat-
form by two assistants, and held up whilst she sang a
ballad in a feeble voice. Fortunately the now elderly
contralto has made a fortune.
A Glasgow waxworks show is doing great business with
the “ Famous Baocyrar Group,” representing the Heir-
apparent and his toadies “fluttering” leather counters
at Tranby Croft. Banker Tummy is officiating at the
head of a green-baized table, and the other members of
the scandal occupy places as per evidence given at the
trial. The real interest of the group lies in the ghastly
stare which young Arthur Wilson fixes upon Gordon-
Cumming, “ whose left hand is placed suspiciously over
a counter near the chalk-line.” Wilson is supposed to be
watching the culprit, intently, and when a wax figure
concentrates its attention as hard as all that the effect
must ho horrible.

Kyrle Bellew writes us from the Green-room Club,
London, under date 3rd October:— Mv Dear Bulletin, —
Your extremely entertaining paper, vi hich 1 never fail to
receive regularly, and which-pardon the apparent
flattery—is far better reading than any of the “funny”
papers on this side the world, generally devotes, and very
kindly, one or two paragraphs in each issue to Mrs. Potter
and myself. Correct reports cannot always be looked for
of people as far distant as the poles, so one cannot view
inaccuracies with a too unkindly eye. In your issue of
August 22, you notice a play by a clever young friend of
mine, named “Jonquille,” and honour me with partici-
pation in its authorship. I cannot allow, however flatter-
ing to myself, the credit for the play to he connected
with ray name in any way. A play often has a plurality
of fathers, but will you allow me, in this instance, to
deny any share in the paternity of Mr. Boothby’s on ray
part. There is a long and unpleasantly suggestive letter
also in the same issue, from a person signing himself
“Trotter Bethune,” of an incident supposed to have been
witnessed by him at 5.30 a.m. on the Essendon line. I
have only to remark in reference to it that “ Trotter
Bethune” was probably suffering from an overnight
debauch, which at that early hour rendered his eyesight
deceptive and himself irresponsible. Yours, 'Kvrle
Bellew.

Poor Vincent West’s divorce case was to have been
heard on the 17th inst. ; death was a speedier and surer
divorcer than the tedious processes of the law courts.
West always caved in without a struggle before the most
trifling complaint, and, no doubt, died a victim to melan-
cholia. Harry Vincent, that was his true name, had the
most mournful eyes that ever looked out of the soul of a
human being, and was one of the most pessimistic of
mortals. Despite his fatalism, he had worked himself up
from being a chorus-singer with Simonsen’s Opera Co.,
and had a future before him.

pin-pointy hand on little square paper, with a kind of

envelope-a head of Thalia, with crossed daggers, and

“The Bulletin” Christinas Number.
The Christmas Number of The Bulletin will be
issued on 17th December.
The Bulletin Christmas Number will embody
original literary contributions from twenty of
the foremost Australian writers, and illustra-
tions from all the representative Australian
artists in black-and-white.
The Bulletin Christmas Number will not con-
sist of cheap imported stories shared with a
hundred other papers throughout the world.
The Bulletin Christmas Number will not be
made up of one prize-story and a prize-
picture and a lot of other matter and
pictures procured gratis from confiding “com-
petitors.”
The Christmas Bulletin, though double the size
of the ordinary weekly issue, will be published
at the usual price, Sixpence.
Agents will please send in their orders before
sth December.
People living where there is no news-agent, can
have the Xmas issue mailed by remitting
Sevenpence in stamps.

Editor Brient, of Sydney D.T., is, we
regret to say, terribly ill of pneumonia—the

gifted artists who has ever visited Australia, is a
grandson of Captain Medwin, the friend and
biographer of Byron and Shelley, and his father
and mother were close friends of Walter Savage
Landor and the Brownings.
When a Sydney Pullman car (carrying
among others, the N.S.W. viceregal party) took
fire the other day on the journey to Melbourne, a
devoted snob (seeing there was no danger, of
course) screamed out: “Oh do save the dear
Governah !” He was heartily laughed at.

stamps.
One of the pleasant features about the
crack-up of the Anglo-Australian Co., is that the
uncalled capital has been pledged to the London
debenture-holders; in other words it has been
pawned and the company only holds the ticket.
Tins uncalled-capital looked a very large and
solid item, and the miserable depositor naturally
felt that he must be safe with such a large security
to fall back upon. Yet in reality there was no
security at all—it ivas all gone up the British
spout. The situation seems very much the same
as that of the embarrassed soft-goods individual,
who pawns his stock, or puts a bill-of-sale upon
his furniture to the detriment of his creditors.
He may use the money thus obtained in paying
his debts ; but still the mere fact that he has
given security to one creditor while the rest are
left out, is described by sundry hard names, and
the misguided person generally gets nasty knocks
or worse, from the Bankruptcy Judge. If this
rule were extended it might do a lot of good to
quite a lot of so-called banks.

Graham Berry lost his deposit at seven
Parliamentary elections.
“If a man smote me on one cheek 1 d
plug him in the eye.”— Copeland, N.S. W. Minister
of Lands,
Victorian Opposition has four leaders—
Gillies, Deakin, “Jingo” Patterson, and Sir
Bryan O’Loghlen.
Pat Perkins, Brisbane’s Boer King, says
that “ anarchy and disorder should be put down
with a firm hand.” Hie !
Parkes’s latest: “I do not know that I
am quite lost to the Legislature of the country.

Prof. Pearson retires from the representa-
tion of Brunswick, in the Victorian Assembly, to
give Berry the chance of the seat.

fou-r lawyers, one merchant, one baker, one ship-
owner, two graziers, and one miner.
Judging by the attendance at Sydney
Woman Suffrage meetings, the Labour Party is
rapidly becoming the Lady Party.
Tasmania's Treasurer expects the present
year to close with a total revenue of £900,000
against an expenditure of £849,507.
The most aggressive being in the world is
a baby. The baby bosses every household. The
New Unionism is the baby of politics.
Of the members of the Victorian Royal
Commission appointed in TO to consider the
question of Australian Federation but Gavan
Duffy, Edward Langton, and Graham Berry are
alive.
Can N.S.W. Treasurer See transfer the,
singular success of his individual operations to his
official work? Out of a few long-shore boats that
could be counted on one’s fingers, John See has
evolved a fine fleet of vessels.
For a considerable time during a recent
sitting of the Victorian Assembly, Sir Bryan
O’Loghlen was the only member on the Opposition
benches. The other Oppositionists were in caucus,
funking the alleged crisis.

Club ; three, for offices ; one, for property ; and
one, for a wharf-interest.

The Melbourne Social Purity Society has
worked up the record of every member of the
Victorian Parliament, and “the report is duly

Dear B ulletin, —Be Woman - suffrage.
There must be only one master in every house. Let
the legislature see that a man and his wife shall be
entitled not to two votes but to one vote between
them, such vote to be polled jointly. Whichever
of the pair happens to be master will control that
vote. In the event of a disagreement no vote to
be recorded. —E.B.
The present N.S.W. Ministry promises
law-reform. Apropos, why should a mere chair-
man of Quarter Sessions have the power of inflict-
ing any sentence short of death ? Cases involving
punishment for life should be tried in the Supreme
Court. In civil matters minor Judges have juris-
diction up to £2OO only, while in criminal cases
they can imprison for life ! A legacy- from Eng-
land, the nation of shopkeepers.
The question of the territorial separation
of North and Central Queensland from South
'Queensland has dropped down a hole in the par-
liamentary floor and won’t be again raised this
session. Meanwhile, Northerners think that their
chances are much improved with Knutsford by
their Parliamentary defeat. Premier Griffith is
grimly reticent on the subject.

The S.A. Assembly have cut down to
£lOO the expenses that may be legally incurred in
contesting a Parliamentary constituency of 2000
electors —this to bo inclusive of all advertising
expenses. Something of the sort is sadly wanted
in N.S.W., where a certain wealthy foreign-trade
member who has hitherto had a sure seat in a
4000-vote constituency, has regularly paid his
■co nmittee a pound per vote on—we don’t say for
—all votes polled.
The Queensland “ Pensions Bill of 1891 ”
provides that any pensioner who becomes a
member of Parliament or a Minister of the Crown
“hall, during his tenure of either position, forfeit
his pension. The Bill is in reality aimed at Chief
Justice Lilley, whose 15 years’ contract of judi-
cial office is about to expire, and who has signified
his willingness to re-enter politics as the Radical
leader. Griffith has never forgiven Lilley for
having once in Parliament styled him “a petti-
fogging lawyer.”

The spineless Sydney Telegraph —that
poor old paper with the haggard expression and
the fallen-in literary stomach —is still wailing.
Its latest complaint is that the Dibbs Govern-
ment, by establishing Protection, will thereby
make Federation impossible. Yet it must
be evident that Protection is the only pos-
sible road to Federation. When Federation
comes, there can be no more inter-provin-
cial tariffs. Therefore, the three Protec-
tionist provinces which abut on N.S. Wales,
assuredly won’t federate so long as N.S.W. is
run on the Foreign-trade system, lor then they
would be swamped by masses of imported shoddy,
landed at Sydney and fired over the border,
thereby entirely destroying the effects of their
own tariffs. They can federate when N.S.W.
goes solid for Protection, but till then it is prac-
tically impossible.
W. O. Hodgkin son, in a recent oration
which he threw like a great slab at the head of
the Queensland Assembly, announced that
“there were hundreds of places in the colony at
the present time where every man could get a fair
wage if he liked to tackle the work.” This is all
the use that the old and slimy variety of
politician is to his country ; ho fires off heavy,
unfinished statements,, and then sits down. For
the miserable Hodgkinson didn’t mention even
one of these places, and he hasn’t mentioned one
of them since. He knew—or, at least, he had
been informed—that there were hundreds of
people almost utterly destitute in Brisbane alone,
and he also knew—unless he was an appalling
perverter of the truth—hundreds of places where
they could all find employment, and he -was too
mean to help his fellow-man by giving the
address. The Bulletin wants to know the
names of these places badly.
The Brazilian Republic seems to be already
falling to pieces for want of one brainy man to
run the show. The Brazilians made a huge mistake
at the very beginning of the movement. When
they wiped out Dorn Pedro as Emperor they ought
to have started the Republic by making the grand
old potentate first President of the new Common-
wealth. He would probably have taken the
post, and beyond the shadow of a doubt be would
have filled it honourably, for —Emperor though he
was— Pedro was the truest republican in all South
America. Also, he was a noble gentleman with
neither spot nor stain on his record, and Brazil
will not grow such another man in ten centuries.
It would have been a grand spectacle _to see
the latest-born Republic start by choosing its
dethroned monarch first chief of the State, but
the new men were small and narrow and common-
place, and so the only country on earth which
could have ventured on such an experiment threw
the chance away.
The N.S.W. Single-tax potentates make a
noise altogether out of proportion to their number.
The local Labour Electoral League rules have
been so drafted-probably through Single-tax
influence—as to allow the membership of people
living outside the district in which a league is
supposed specially to operate. Thus, for instance,
a man may belong to Newtown L.E.L. without
residing at Newtown, or being an elector of that
constituency. Nor is any limit placed upon the
number of leagues to which he may belong, and
so it happens that the Sinode-tax bosses each
belong to several leagues, have a lot to say
wherever they go—and, being always “on the
job,” carry about a floating majority with them.
When they visit the country they, of course, don’t
talk too much Single-tax to the farmers, among
whom they appear as Freetraders only, and when
they find it judicious, they talk Labour without
any reference to either Single-tax or Freetrade.
The way to keep these bogus maj irities in their
proper place, is to allow people to belong only to
the league of the district in which they reside.

trade Camp, could all join hands, and form a strong party
under the leadership of a man like G. H. Reid.

this combination a “Federated People’s Party !”
Baronet Clarke opened Jus mouth once
(except for yawning) during the “dual vote”
discussion in the Vic. Legislative Council. A
succession of ponderous old members had been
enlarging on the rights of Proputty, and the
absurdity of reckoning one whole landlord as one
plain ordinary man, when Clarke took the floor
and fired the bewildering suggestion that tenants
ought to have two votes, because many of them
are “ superior men to their landlords.” Then the
baronet sat down amidst an awesome silence. He
evidently meant well, and was doubtless making a
generous allusion to his own tenants, but the
other old gentlemen felt that their exalted
colleague was getting socialistic and obscure. He
was opening up problems. The man who owned
one house and rented the house next door would
evidently want four votes. Also the lodger who
pays a landlord half his rent for a couple of un-
furnished rooms would claim one vote and a half,
and the boarders at every hash-house would
certainly howl for an extra decimal fraction of a
vote apiece. The matter was altogether too deep
for Proputty to find the bottom.

Jordan “If the widows of those who serve the
State in Parliament, and get good salaries for
doing so, are to receive grants of .£2OOO, tlier
corresponding grants should be made to th ( '
widows of those who have had to provide the
wherewithal for the payment of the said salaries
and special votes ”

In Berlin most of the tobacconists have
the telephone laid on, and all you have to do if
you want to use it is to purchase a penny cigar
and you may talk away to your heart’s content!
This facility has its disadvantages—for the man
at the other end of the wire. The facility f or
dunning tardy debtors is too apparent, and there
is just the chance that all your angry creditor says
may be overheard. Fancy, say, Friedman’s or
Rogalsky’s being laid on to the N.S.W. Lands
Office ! —or Marks Hermann on to the Melbourne
Education Department.

Writes our London correspondent :—“A
friend of the deceased ‘ Uncrowned King’ asserts
me that Parnell never thoroughly believed in
Gladstone’s conversion to Home Rule. He never
trusted the G.0.M., who, he thought had too
superficial a knowledge of Irish history, which ho
did not commence to study until he saw that the
Irish party would have to be reckoned with both
in and out of the House of Commons. It was this
firm disbelief which prompted him to take up the
gage so warmly when Mr. Gladstone so readily
availed himself of the O’Shea business to insist on
Parnell’sretirement. I have every reason to believe
this, for my informant is one of the few men who
has ever been admitted even to the slightest in-
timacy with the remarkable man who has just
passed away.”
W. C Wentworth inclined to the belief
that the Government of the day in distributing the
rewards for the discovery of gold in N.S.W.
should have allotted Tom, Lister, and Simpson
Davison proportions of the votes, instead of giving
Hargraves so large a sum. Davison might, by all
the equities of the case, have certainly come in
for a larger share than Hargraves, and yet he never
received a penny, neither did Tom or Lister who
found the gold which Hargraves showed to the
Government in proof of his contention that he
had made the discovery. The currently-accepted
notion as to Hargraves’ responsibility for the dis-
coveries will have to be knocked on the head, like
many other notions similarly given currency to by
lazy journalists and lazier “authors.” Davison
was a man of very remarkable attainments,
scientific and literary, and his letters to the
Empire— particularly No. 8 of the series—were as
well-done a piece of satire as has ever appeared in
Australia.
It is singular, seeing how much depends
on good health, that a little more pains is not
taken to impress on the people a few simple rules
for its preservation. Thus, bad drainage is the
main cause of typhoid fever, which kills 20,000
people in England yearly. Small and low bed-
rooms engender consumption and loss of vitality
unless well ventilated, not only in the day-time,
but at night. An ill-ventilated bedroom is a
frequent cause of sleeplessness. Children are
especially sufferers from badly-ventilated bed-
rooms. That is the chief cause why children of
the poor look so sickly. It may be said that
people have a right to be filthy. So they have,
unless they are an active annoyance and danger
to their neighbours. For that reason there is
greater logic in prosecuting a dirty than a drunken
man. Where disease heralds its approach by such
signs as indigestion, headache, neuralgia, tired
aching limbs and other well-known symptoms, a
course of Clements Tonic will quickly restore the
normal health as instanced in the case of His
Honor Judge Miller, who writes :—“ Court House,
Winton, Queensland, June 10, ’9l.—Last Decem-
ber while travelling from Muttaburra to-Winton,
I was suddenly seized with a violent attack of
vomiting and diarrhoea. On my arrival at Winton,
Mr. Campbell (of Corfield and Fitzmaurice) per-
suaded me to take Clements Tonic ; one dose
relieved me. I continued to take it for two days,
at the expiration of which I was completely re-
covered, and I have much pleasure in testifying
to the beneficial effects I experienced from taking
it. —Granville George Miller, Judge of the Central
District Court, Winton.”
Take Ayer’s Fills, and bo cured. Misery is a
mild word to describe the sufferings of body and
mind, caused by habitual constipation. A
moderate use of Ayer's Pills will invariably
regulate the bowels.
That’s logic, sir, and so’s the bit
That I am going to submit.
I work as hard as any man—
That is, I work whene’er I can.
And what is more, as i contend,
Is—all the cash I get, I spend.
My money’s always got full wings,
And flies away in taxed things.
In baooa, tea, and “ mountain dew
In fact, I prop the revenue.
And when I die, my widow will
Be pushed to pay my coffin bill.
She’ll have, when I am gone to dust,
To earn before she eats her crust.
A bonus, then, is fairly due
To one who props the revenue.
And, lest my widow meets mischance,
I’ll take her bonus in advance.

Old Judae Cope, for 30 years of the Vic-
torian County Court, who retired three years ago.
is dead at last. He was a man of gentle humour,
and a victim of the shent-per-shent persuasion.
We sincerely trust that the amiable old man has
not gone to the bosom of Abraham. Nothing
would disgust him more than to meet anyone-
bearing such a name.

Labouchere on Disraeli :
This astute and unscrupulous Hebrew had laid himself
out to obtain the Queen’s favour. On his first admission
to her Court it is related of him that he assured her that
he owed his success as au orator to a careful study of the
Prince Consort’s speeches, all of which he committed to
heart; and his entire subsequent intercourse with her
was conducted in a like vein of Oriental hyperbole.
Lord Sheffield, who heads the English
team of cricketers now at Adelaide, is a little
podgy man, almost as broad as he is long; the
crowd laughed hugely when he waddled across
Adelaide Oval with two or three of his sturdy
players. The Earl is clean-shaven with the ex-
ception of a straggly, half-moon Irish beard under
the chin. He spends plenty of money, and
threatens to be very popular.
Old Jock Winter, sire of Winter-Irving,
M.L.C. (Vic-), was a sturdy old warrior with no
frills, and hated “dressy ” men. At his home on
the Devil’s River he was once interviewed by a
man with a well-waxed moustache and looking as
if he had just stepped out of a fashion-plate. The
meetincr was not of a cordial rature, and, on the
beau taking his departure, he passed some remark
which aroused Jock’s ire. Jock chased him, caught
him, knelt on his chest, deprived him of his
moustache with a foot-rotting knife, and was all
smiles and sunshine for more than a week after.

An utterly indecisiv e man ; a man with no mind of his
own. Very well learned, of course, in bugs and beetles,
and a most interesting talker on all matters of natural
history. Yes, a charmingly interesting man, a good con-
versationalist, a man just suited, for instance, for a lady’s
afternoon tea-party
We remember reading something stronger than
this. It was Emin Pasha’s opinion of Stanley.
David Syme prints in his own paper an
advertisement given him by Patchett Martin,
writing in Liverary Opinion ;
Only two so-called Australian writers have displayed
profound erudition or original philosophical capacity, the
late Dr. Hearn, author of the Aryan Household and
Philology, and Mr. David Syme, who has so daringly
crossed swords with the Darwinians in his suggestive
work On the Modification of Organisms. But neither
Mr. Syme nor Dr. Hearn saw Australia until after he had
reached manhood.
Modest and tasteful, ain’t it?
Stanley has deducted 99 per cent, from
Phil. Robinson’s graphic account of how he wrote
up and rounded “Through the Dark Continent,”
from the dictation of that apotheosised nigger-
slayer. Speaking at a Savage Club dinner, Stan-
ley, the leading savage of the evening, observed,
fixing his eye upon the man at whom he was talk-
ing, “There is a gentleman present who claims to
have assisted me in my literary work. I take the
opportunity of telling that gentleman to his face
that ho is’ a liar.” Phil R. was the gentleman
present, and he swallowed the soft impeachment
without a murmur.
Dr. Rutter, of Parramatta, N.S.W., has a
deal to answer for. Out with a hunting-party in
Warwickshire some seventy years ago, rain coming
on they adjourned to a roadside cottage. There a
sturdy youngster was continually making" signs to
his mother when he wanted to attract her atten-
tion. On enquiry she stated that he was deaf and
dumb, but the good doctor, who knew better,
called round the next day and cut a rigid muscle
that prevented the tongue from wagging. That
child was ex-Premier Parkes. It is only fair to say
Parkes denies this and asserts that he had the use
of all his faculties at birth. He ought to know.
He was there.
0. Hutton, who thinks he remembers
Lord Salisbury as a young man on the Victorian
goldfields, writes: —
I think he was working at Golden Point, near Castle-
maine. There were two young fellows, and they lived in
a very comfortable framed tent, and dug a hole and
bottomed it about 20 or 30 yards away from their tent.
What made it so remarkable was that they had a servant
in livery to carry the picks and shovels from the tent to
their claim. They were not rigged out like miners,
having white shirts. The diggers when they passed used
to sing out “ Joe ! ” but they took no notice of it. I sup-
pose that must have been Salisbury. The lime was either
December, 1851, or January, 1852, shortly after we arrived
on the diggings, and like unexperienced diggers they
sunk their claim on solid ground away from the lead of
gold.
Bir Richard Temple, the man with the
largest, reddest, bulgiest, and most shiny nose in
England, is going to retire from the House of
Commons at the end of the present Parliament.
He was once Governor of Bombay, but came home
when Disraeli was in his zenith to hunt for the
office of Viceroy at Calcutta. As a preliminary
he called on the eminent Hebrew, and was ushered
into the drawing-room. Lady Beaconsfield was
there; she was weak and nervous, and when she
saw this fearful proboscis coming in she shrieked
and fainted. Disraeli heard the row and coming
down the stairs ten at ajump he found Sir Richard
hovering _ over the insensible lady and sniffing
in an agitated fashion. They were never quite
friendly after that, and Temple never became a
Governor again.
It is well known that between Hargraves,
the alleged gold-discoverer, and the Rev. W. B.
Clarke, the famous geologist, there was no love
lost. Hargraves took every opportunity to depre-
cate Clarke’s judgment as “mere theory,” “book
knowledge only,” and so forth. The antagonism
of the two had been smouldering from the date of
Hargraves’ first application to the Government for
a reward (3rd April, 1851), but burst into flame
when Hargraves followed Clarke in inspecting the
Araluendistrict. Clarkedescribes tbegold-bearing
characteristics of the region in the most glowing
terms and prophesied a rich future for it. Har-
graves, in his report, sneered at Clarke’s judgment,
and “ventured to assert the field would prove
next to worthless.” Clarke’s judgment was con-
firmed almost to the very letter, as the Araluen
deposits were rich, abundant, and of considerable
depth.
Sir Janies Martin used to claim, with some
degree of pride, that his “Australian Sketch
Book,” published in 1838, was the first literary
production “ that ever emanated from the pen of
an individual educated in Australia.” He was
wrong; the first purely literary work, pub-
lished here, was by a locally-educated native,
Charles Tompson, who subsequently became
clerk of_ the Legislative Assembly, N.S.W.
Tompson’s book is a bibliographical curiosity.
It is the only work printed with the imprint
“Australia” —not Sydney, N.S.W., but simply
“ Australia ’’—and this at a time when the official
designation of the continent at large was still
“ New Holland.” Young Tompson’s father kept
a beer-shop in George-street, and the young poet
used to squat on the bar-counter and read his
verses to the customers. It is not recorded that
the habit drove the drinkers away and his father
pi to insolvency.
“ Blind’' Jackson, of Bathurst-, N.S.W.,
died of influenza the other day. He had been
stone blind for 42 years, and a familiar feature of
Bathurst for more than half-a-century.
King Jackey, one of the last of the
Monaro (N.S.W.) blacks, died at Cooma, cn 7th
inst., of influenza, at the ancient age of “nine
feller big feller floods; plenty feller little feller
floods.”
The Age’s latest and lengthiest attack upon
The Bulletin appeared in last Saturday’s issue.
For this occasion only the deplorable example of
successful penny journalism sank so low as to
lickspittle the mendacious parson lately in charge
of Melbourne D.T., and the spectacle of Ananias
patting the dog that has bitten him a thousand
times is absolutely painful. Says David Syme, or
his fully-instructed myrmidon; “Mr. Fitchett
pledges his word (!) that they (Bulletin admirers)
don’t number one in a thousand of the newspaper
reading public, and after a cursory glance at its
contents we can quite believe it,” which means, in
unintentional English, that the Age believes The
Bulletin. Bad grammar, however, is not worth
noticing in a daily paper. Now, when the Age
professes to believe Fi-tchett’s lie that orthodox
journal simply lies again in its unclean teeth, but
when, as the result of a “cursory glance” the
lumbering animal proceeds to quote long extracts
from The Bulletin it goes out of its dull,
stupid way to hold itself up to ridicule. Not one
“ cursory glance,” but years of cursory considera-
tion has the Age given to the paper which at last
makes the wounded beast howl in impotent rage.
And the impotency of this anger is comic to con-
template. Quoting from a Bulletin leader, the
Age takes a carefully-perverted old figure of speech
in the same solemn spirit with which Sir James
Macß. accepted the harmless joke of the friend,
who said “All hail, Macßain !” “ It’s all hail,
Macbeth, I’m thinkin’,” replied the ponderous
President of the Legislative Council. In like
manner the slow-witted “morning dreary”
scratched its fat head and murmured—“lt ought
to be loins, ye ken,” upon coming across the
Bulletin’s reference to the Single-tax League
“girding up its brains for the fray.” David Syme
is Scotch —that’s all.

Society
The average monthly interments at Rcok-
wood, Sydney’s great cemetery, are 140. This
month people have been buried at the rate of 35
a day—and 35 x 31 equals 1085 ! Forty-seven
gravediggers have been working day and night,
and it has frequently been found impossible to get
men for the work.
Doctors have also been scarce in Sydney, arid
Mr. Bruck, the medical agent, has offered double
the ordinary fees without securing the services of
enough qualified men to take up the running for
his disabled clients. However, had the Bruck
been able to get all the doctors be asked for, it is
quite likely that the city would have found itself
50 per cent, short of gravediggers.
* # #
The sudden flight of Gov. Jersey from
Melbourne the other week was not to be wondered
at, for he is naturally a retiring sort of earl, and
the frequent indispositions of his vice-regal hostess
embarrassed him beyond measure. Theatres are
the only places where my lady Hopetoun doesn’t
appear to “come over faint” of late. Behind the
curtains of a private box at the Bijou or Princess’
the fair daughter of Erin can sit and look amiable
for three hours at a stretch, but most other enter-
tainments are found too trying. A couple of
months back she thought to enjoy a nice, long
Sunday morning at the Anglican Cathedral. It
was not to be, however. Before the service was
fairly on its legs Lady H. had fled from the
sacred building, with an aide tumbling after her.
She didn’t even come back for a minute to hear
the shepherd call down a blessing on our beloved
Queen and family.
* * *
The other day a Sydney man was divorced
by his wife, the evidence against him being bis
own admissions and the testimony of a bosom
friend of his, with whom he walked down the
street in quite a friendly way a day or two after-
wards. Two nights after the Judge had pro-
nounced a rule nisi, the divorced man was seen
to hand his ex-wife into a cab at the theatre-door
in a most amiable manner. Toujours la politesse!
* # #
A lady—one of the fortunate drawers in
Tattersall’s sweep—on calling for her cheque
(£9OOO in the second sweep) said : “ I and another
lady went ’alves in it just for a bit of fun.”
When asked if she had laid anything off she
replied, “I don’t know what that is.” She also
remarked that her husband was returning from
the country on the morrow, in total ignorance of
the fortune that awaited him. What a blissful
re-union ! What a more than heavenly jewel such
a wife would seem !
• * «
Ajiropos of the attempt to disrupt the
N.S.W. Art Society—it is as well to here mention
an eminently weak spot in the armour of that
body. Leading members and chief exhibitors of
the society form the hanging committee for the
annual show, which affords a colour of evidence
that the pictures are arranged on the “ claw-me-
claw-thee ” principle. Why should not the work
of hanging be deputed to some uninterested out-
sider—say one of the local picture-dealers ? It is
impossible for exhibitors to strike fair balances
between themselves and other people. No man
is a competent judge in his own cause.
* • #
Duquesne, who got jealous of Sara Bern-
hardt’s chcr ami, Darmont, and was dismissed at
New York for “ acting grotesquely on the stage,”
had been suffering from the heart-trouble which,
sooner or later, seizes on all men who become
friendly with Sara. When The Bulletin pub-
lished a rumour during the Bernhardt's stay in
Sydney that she had married Darmont, Duquesne
felt very bad indeed. Darmont also professed to
feel very bad indeed, and proclaimed in one of the
clubs —late at night—that he was going to horse-
whip The Bulletin.

The accounts of how W. J. Clarke (Sir)
met with his hard financial “knocks” vary con-
siderably, but to the fact that the bald-headed
Bart, is (in a Crcesian sense only, mind you) “hard
up ” there is plenty of expert evidence flying
around Melbourne. A comparative scarcity of
ready money drives him to Europe, where he will
escape for a while the necessity of saying “ No
hateful to him after years of putting his hand in
his pocket—to the people who had come to regard
old butcher Clarke’s first-born as a free loan-office
and a subscription-dispensary. Although political
economy kicks hard against men like W.J.C. he
is at least Australian in his respect for the soil
which produces his unearned income, and lie isn’t
sneaking “ home ” for the purpose of setting up as
an Anglo-Colonial and buying the occasional
smiles of bong-tong with Cargantuan dinners,
like Salmon Wilson and other members of the
bloated “absentee” brotherhood.

# * *
The Free Presbyterian Synod of Victoria
has passed, amongst others, the following reso-
lution :
This Synod agrees to record solemnly an acknowledg-
ment of the rod in God's hands by which the recent wide-
spread malady called influenza lias swept so many in this
colony and some of the members of this church to their
long home.
In fact, the Synod agreed to give the Deity a
solemn receipt for its chastisement. In the next
breath the Synod recorded its—
Sense of desert of God’s afflictive visitations by Church
and State.
Which requires much untangling. Does the Synod
accuse the Almighty of afflicting sneezers by
Church and State ? Or does it record the Church’s
and the State’s deserts of catarrh ? Do Pro-
vidential “ afflictive” visitations invariably come
per snuffle? Again, if the Almighty is epidemic
influenza, is Beelzebub the antidotal perchloride
of mercury? Under the circumstances, there is
more anti-pneumonic virtue in a shilling bottle of
eucalyptus oil that in the whole continent-full of
Presbyterian Synods.

It was the influenza microbe, and not the
sea-serpent, that Bishop Kennion found dead on
the beach at Coffin Bay, S.A.
. # # *
A fqvv days ago an old Sydney identity
(William Dean) died, according to doctor’s certi-
ficate, of influenza. He was a solitary, withered
old man, who livfed no one knew how—no one
cared. He had a few thousands in hard cash at
one of the banks, but his solicitor found that his
property amounted to £40,000. The old man had
lived alone for the past 30 years. A char-woman
who attended - his house weekly, visited the
lawyer one morning and informed him that her
master was dying really of starvation, that he
had not even a bed in his house to die on, and that
he would not let her buy a glass of milk for him.
The man had lived and died for his gold. He
would come into the bank, drenched, for he was
too mean to buy an umbrella. He was a remorse-
less enemy to himself, living the life of a starved,
hunted rat, and, with the exception of £5OO, he
has left all his property to the Church. R.I.P.

A Newcastle (N.S.W.) lady was scalded
to death lately at a big Sydney hotel, through the
accidental or careless turning-on of a steam-tap in
a bath-room, but the coroner does not seem to
have been consulted in the matter.
# # *
Presiding at the Womanhood Suffrage
League, Lady Windeyer said women wish to be
helpers of men. Nothing noteworthy in that.
Every chaperone takes the same view of the sub-
ject and helps her daughter to the very richest
men available.
# # *
On 9th Nov. a Sydney florist displayed a
Prince of Wales plume with the revised motto,
“ Ich bedien ” (I follow suit), in place of the old
“ Ich dien ” (I serve).
The other day. in England, a man—not ex-
Gov. Carington—dislocated his jaw whileyawning,
andnearly died through it. A man named Westall,
living at Ormondville (M.L.), has just gone one
better : he was a man who, whenever his jaw
found anything to do, did it with all his might,
and he yawned with such fervour that he dislo-
cated his shoulder, and had to walk about eight
miles over a rough country to get it set. He
intends to give up yawning after this, lest he
should dislocate his brains next time.
# # *
Candid was Rev. Steel before Melbourne
Presbytery last week, saying, in other words,
“ How can we ask the working man to contribute
to our Infirm Ministers’ Fund, when the minister
gets twice as much wages ?” And why can’t the
clergy go into benefit-societies?
# # *
The daughter of an English clergyman is
alleged to have developed a wonderful capacity
for finding gold by means of the divining-rod.
She would be a useful young lady to stand outside
of the average Sydney land-bank at a large salary.
# # *
It was in a certain Maoriland city that
Louis Lohr thought to tighten the silken bonds
between church and stage by securing one of the
Cathedral chorister boys for Williamson’s Juvenile
Opera Company. Overtures were made to the
parents of the lad, and his sweet soprano was in a
fair way to quit the sanctuary for the footlights
when the Dean and Chapter came along and burst
up the ungodly project entirely. Lohr made a
feeble attempt to prove that a “ star ” juvenile
from the Cathedral would serve as a missionary
to the other youngsters, and would be able to open
rehearsal with a choice selection from the best
anthems. The Dean turned a deaf ear to this line
of argument, and the Bishop called down Heaven’s
wrath upon the unfortunate business-manager,
also upon everybody having authority over the
company, until they finally departed from the city
with a full treasury, but a firm determination to
look for no more talent behind an altar. The
dignified clergy brook no interference with any
chorister whose voice helps to fill the tabernacle
and is worth, perhaps, a tenner a week to thf
offertory.

The Australian Auxiliary Squadron cap-
hired every sip of liquor during the visit to
Thursday Island. The isolated settlement was
drunk as dry as a lime-kiln, and there wasn’t
enough grog left to anoint a grain of the sad sea
sand,

of Brisbane fire brigade, who dropped dead of heart
disease, was a brother-in-law to Morell Mackenzie,
the celebrated throat specialist, and was to have
been married in January next to a Sydney lady.

There is a grim and ghastly joker on a
Newcastle journal. When the festive, soi-disant
“Dr. Roland Danvers Brin ton ” was captured in
Coalopolis on a charge of stealing jewellery, and
for which he is now doing five years in Darling-
hurst Gaol, he asked the pressman to get huu
something to read. The journalist procured him
David Christie Murray’s “He FeT Among
Thieves.”
* « #
A London magazine lately offered one
guinea for the best definition of “a good husband.”
The following won the prize :
One who is attentive without being exacting, lover-like
without being jealous, generous without being reckless,
proud without being vain, tender without being weak,
sensible without being cold, refined without being over-
fastidious, and man enough never to forget that lie
married with his eyes open, Such is a model husband.
We understand. It pans out thus : —Attentive to
requests without exacting reasons, consistently
uxorious, and too stupid to know a cicisbeo from
an ox; generous to the baby, by buying toys with
the pocket-money which reckless men spend in
tobacco ; proud of his wife, and not vain of him-
self ; tender enough husband, and strong enough
father, to hush and to carry the squawker at
midnight; sensible enough to perceive when
common-sense might chill, refined enough to
remark when the dinner is well done, and not
over-fastidious when there is nothing in the house;
and man enough to always play the part of the
fox who had lost his tail. Yes ! Alas, fo weary
man ! this is the model type.
* # *
Reflectively said a Sydney junior barrister
the other day: “What is the use of Divine
Providence ? It sends the influenza and the in-
fluenza sends for nobody except poor old Teddy
Bennett.”
* * #
A Maoriland gentleman who bolted some
time ago with another man’s wife, returned un-
expectedly the other day, and committed a brutal
assault on the injured husband. He offered no
explanation of his conduct, but it is presumed
that he was enraged with the other man for not
overtaking him in his flight, and wresting the
lady from him ere it was too late.

Police-magistrate Pinnock, of Brisbane,
has been horrified to learn that the Sydney police
took the wedding-ring off ’ the finger of Mrs.
Johnston, arrested in Sydney on a Brisbane
warrant, charging her with fraudulent bankruptcy.
“I am quite certain that the detectives here

a Sydney institution, which has gone into liquida
tion, will insist on laiowing how many directors
or ex-directors were given big overdrafts by their

The prospect of gaol and little things like
that is generally supposed to deter people from
committing crime. The Bulletin doesn’t believe
a word of it, but as the general public appear to
be under the delusion that punishment is a
deterrent, the N.S.W. Government ought to spend
if necessary i? 10,000 in following round the world
Bell and Davies, the two cheeky and so far
successful scoundrels who travelled so long and
so successfully on the Turkey carpet in their gor-
geous suite of offices.

THE JADE I LOVE.
(Song after Hamilton Aide.)
I love a jade with pencilled eye,
Who acts at the the-a-ter,
The gold that she is making fly
I wheedle from my mater ;
You would be shocked if you could twig
Her perched upon my knee ;
I'm twenty, but I’m none too big— .
She’s stout and forty-three.
I'm twenty, but I’m none too big—
She’s stout and forty-three.
She thinks me very young, I feel
She treats me like her poodle,
And when to kiss her hand I kneel
She merely murmurs “ noodle ! ”
I’ve bought the smartest horse and gig
Her faked-up eye could see ;
I’m twenty, but I’m none too big
She’s stout and forty-three.
( Repeat.)
X fear she is inconstant, too
That she has more admirers ;
She tells me tales that are not true,
For girls are such Sapphiras.
She doesn’t seem to care a fig
When I bring out a D. ;
I’m twenty, but I’m none too big—
She’s stout an’d forty-three.
(Repeat.)
And when my back is bent with age,
And I no more can tarry
To pick up charmers from the stage
(Ere then, of course, I’ll marry),
My whisky 1 shall calmly swig
That jade won’t worry me ;
I’ll buy her, though, a golden wig
The day she’s eighty-three—
Yes, Maud shall have a golden wig
The day she’s eighty-three.
Melbourne.

At Adelaide, Mr. James Dargan got two
months’ hard labour for continuous attacks upon
his paralysed wife, whom he “ kicked and beat
for two hours until she was covered with blood.”
Fun is cheap in the City of Saints.

There is to be some fun over the re-
hearing of the well-advertised Sydney commission
case, Woods v. Armstrong, which lets people
behind the scenes of the North Sydney Tramway
and Investment Company. Lord Hindlip is to
come from England and give evidence, and then
we shall hear what we shall hear.

The latest sensation concerns a prominent
solicitor and leading light of clubland, who is
alleged to have made improper use of certain deeds
which he borrowed from a bank in his professional
capacity. The alleged delinquent, who is closely
related' to a rich politician, lias resigned the
membership of the two aristocratic clubs, and
retired from the legal firm in which he was a
junior partner.

cords is when a parson bursts out with the whoop-
ing-cough in the middle of his sermon.
With Prince Albert died the British

The life of a railway lengthsman in Central
Queensland :—He has to walk the whole distance
of his length—the lengths vary from five to eight
miles—every morning before the first train, which
means.before breakfast, “ there and back, or from
10 to 16 miles, and examine carefully all the line,
especially dog-spikes, fish-plates, fences, and tele-
graph-poles and lines.” That done, he has to work
all day long in a broiling sun, or a pouring rain,
with a beater or shovel. At night he herds with
his family in a two-roomed kennel that lets in the
weather everyway. These kennels are 24ft. by
10ft., gross measurement, each room being not big
enough to gleefully swing a cat in ! Yet 10 to 11
persons—parents and children—have often to herd
m these disgraceful shanties. There are no out-
offices whatever. Yet the Q. Railway Commis-
sioners charge their wretched slaves 2s. fid. per
week rent for the hovels.
The imported Baldwin engines, like most
imported things in N.S.W., have proved to be
shoddy. They cost as much as engines locally
manufactured, tin y are huge and cumbersome
so huge that the platforms have had to be shifted
at many country stations to prevent them scraping
away great chunks of the premises, tlmy are too
heavy for the wheels, their parts are a wonderful
series of misfits, and they seldom get to the end
of a journey without breaking down miserably.
Baldwin is a good many thousand miles away,
however, so that no one can sue him for damages
with much show of success, and the country must
put up with the job as best it can.
An artist agreed to paint the picture of a
saint for the refectory-wall of a convent, at a
rniserab'y low price, provided that his meals were
given him during the work. The only food the
convent supplied was bread, onions, and water.
The artist said nothing, but proceeded serenely
with tiie wo k. At last came the ceremony of
publicly unveiling the picture. In the presence of
the holy friars the artist removed the curtain, and
revealed —a first-class back-view of the saint.
“ What does this mean?” indignantly demanded
the prior. “Father,” said the artist, meekly, “ I
had to paint him this way, because the saint
couldn’t bear the smell of onions.”
Eighteen times in the course of last month
the Sydney Echo got in a rage and wanted to say
something dreadfully severe about Tins Bulletin,
and each time it had to relieve its feelings by
calling it “a weakly paper.” It was the only
humorous and crushing observation the Echo
could find ; in all that huge building there is only
one joke, and it isn’t up to much, and into the
bargain it is borrowed. The Bulletin invented
that joke years ago, and gave it up because it
seemed an inferior article, and now its own left-
off observation keeps turning up again like an old
sin that won’t be bmied. Be sure your joke will
find you out. When you give up using it your-
self someone else will come and tell it to you. By
the way, what’s the matter with “the weakly
daily-?”
The Sydney tailors are out on strike, and
the employers, so far, refuse a conference. The
pliers of needle and thread ask a similarity of pay
at all the houses. Some of the largest pay only lOd.
an hour, and the men wish to ri-e to the shilling
THE SHADY SIDE OF THE PARK.
an hour paid elsewhere. This does not imply that
good and bad, slow and quick workmen will be
equally remunerated. At present each tailor is
allowed so many hours—in every case notoriously
inadequate—to make each garment, and is paid
Is or lOd. an hour for the time allegedly occupied
in making it, a method of calculation equivalent
to payment by piecework. The tailors ask, there-
fore, that the time-log shall bo to some extent
assimilated to that of the London workshops,
where the allowance is much longer. Average
earnings in Sydney, they say, are 38s weekly.
The pale-faced Cockney who doesn’t pin
his faith to one particular London journal and
derive all his “ facts ” from the same source,
must, by this time, have formed some kind of
opinion of his own concerning the influence of The
Bulletin. In the Fortnightly Review he finds
it treated as the weekly Bible of Young Australia,
and in the Contemporary Review he sees it
summed up, by Crusty Murray, as “ the most
mischievous paper in the world.” Anon he rubs
his eyes after staring at the vermilion lie of
Parson Fitchett, printed by the Pall Mall
Gazette, that the apocryphal “great ivecklies,
taken together, have 500 readers where The Bul-
letin has one,” after which an ex-Mayor of Ade-
laide (James Shaw) tells him, in St. James’
Budget, “it is perfectly true that The Bulletin
is a paper which is widfly read.” Then the
Cockney, putting two and two together, and
having read Stead to the effect that The Bulletin
is “a clever, wicked paper,” begins to strike a
vague idea of the situation for himself, and
arrives, in the first instance, at an inevitable con-
clusion that Fitchett is booked for the wrath to
come. Murray and the ex-Mayor person, both
being loaded up with reverence for the Royal
Family as an institution, are naturally maligners
of The Bulletin, but not having entered into
holy orders, they seem to entertain conscientious
scruples about libelling its circulation.

A king got lost the other day in Paris.
Young Alexander of Servia— aged 1!) or so —went
mysteriously a-missing from the opera, where he
had gone with his father, ex-monarch Milan.
Milan had just stepped out to drink with another
king or two, and when he came back his son was
a vanished potentate. Then there was a fearsome
row, it being taken for granted that Alexander
h;ul been abducted, and the detectives were put
on the track. As it happened, however, they had
seen a good many kings, and knew pretty well
where they went to when they got lost. They
simply bolted round among the immoral cafes,
and at the most immoral one of the lot they found
the stray Anointed rather drunk, and violently
embracing a barmaid.
The Orange societies held a demonstration
in Sydney lately to celebrate something, or lo
keep green the memory of some corpse or other.
Preacher Madgwick was there, and he took the
opportunity to offer these remarks ;
Touching the Prince of Wales, the heir-apparent had
reached his jubilee year. It, was a common thing to
hear the Prince spoken of disparagingly, more particu-
larly since the Tranby Croft incident. Whilst lie (Mr.
Madgwick) deprecated gambling, he jet believed that at
the bottom the Prince of Wales was a sound and good
man—a man who loved his people, and who would he
willing to sacrifice himself for the people whom he con-
templated being called to rule over.
It is a gratifying thing to know that Walts is
sound at the bottom. Only he is dreadfully
decayed at the top, and that is just what the
trouble is about. The country wants a man who
is healthy at both ends.
There is a whole lot of humour about the
unchartered bank, especially when it bursts, and
all the facts come out. Probably the most
amusing institution of the lot is the Federal
Reversionary I. and F. Co. This distracted com-
pany held a meeting last Saturday to define its
position, and explain where the cash had gone,
and why it went there, and the manager, Mr.
Miller, told a tearful story, from which it
appeared that he had had much heavy digging to
do. Certain old-time directors had power to
draw cheques without his signature, and when-
ever they wanted money they simply drew a
cheque accordingly. Then the manager had to
serve on a jury, and while he was away the
directors rushed the office, and carried off £250.
When he came back the board turned up again,
meaning to carry off the rest of the cash, but Mr.
Miller told them they would have to climb over
his dead body to get at the safe. Further, he
tore off his coat and cast himself into the breach,
and “ stoushed ” the board so hard in the eye
that it fled without the money. They got a new
lot of directors after that, but apparently the old
board with the ruined optic never settled up.

Now and Then.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Now we see a mortal tremble, cringe and stoop to
brother man ;
Now we see the little children, thin and pinched,
and white and wan ;
Noiv we see thin, careworn women, see their
stunted bodies small,
See them nurse their sickly babies, who will work
when they can crawl;
Now arise up all around us hellish sights which
meet our gaze—
Sights which though noiv lightly looked at will be
loathed in later days :
Of the worker slaving, toiling, from the morn
until the night;
Delving in dark, filthy caverns when God’s sun
gives out its light;
Tasting air but grudging given, while allround
him seems to teethe—
Air, which flows as free as heaven, not for one,
but all to breathe ;
Slaving on through calm and tempest, slaving on
in .earth and sea ;
Never thinking, never striving for the time that is
to be—
He has got no time for thinking how the world
might be made gay.
Only his the time for thinking how to keep the
wolf away—
Slaving where the fire burns fiercest, where the
cold is felt indeed ;
Slaving ever, ever, ever, slaving for another’s
greed.
Now we see those sights around us : misery which
fattens lust;
They the products of a system which must crumble
into dust.
And the true man’s face grows weary thinking of
his fellow men—
Thinking how they toil in day-time, how they
rest in loathsome den :
Such a contrast lo the beauty o! the times that
will be —then !
Then shall all mankind stand upright—stand so
brave, and fair, and free ;
Then shall children, starved no longer, play and
laugh iu merry glee ;
Then shall happy wives and mothers wish no more
that they were dead,
Fight no more life’s dreary battle, live a life of
peace instead ;
Then shall rise up all around us, sights—when
greed and shame depart
Glorious sights, in which no longer stone usurps
the place of heart :
Of all mankind’s chances equal : equal chance of
head or hand,
When each man shall be a free man, slave to none
throughout the land.
Of the rapine and the murder which has shrieking
flown away —
The same rapine, the same murder which are
everywhere to-day ;
Of the earth which is a heaven : and the filthy,
loathsome hell
Which is now this woild of sorrow, and in which
the workers dwell,
Is fur ever lost and vanished, and which, like a
fearsome dream
From the memory once banished, flies, and never
more is seen;
Then we’ll see these sights around us : wealth no
more from squalor wrung ;
Then we’ll know that from our sj'stem, fair and
bright the New has sprung.
And the true man’s eyes grow radiant when this
world of grief is gone
And this earth becomes a heaven for the gods to
dwell upon.
WILL WARREN.
Sydney, Nov., 1891.

Docs the embezzler, plnngefully, ever
make a turf rise, and save liis bacon, and become
reformed, and live happily ever after?
The Civil servants in the Northern Terri-
tory are split up into two factions. There are
140 Europeans and 105 Asiatics employed, and
they don’t recognise each other outside office
hours.
Last Call shares have been offering in
Sydney at half-a-crown, without buyers. A lot
of very clever people are left in possession of reams
of scrip, purchased at from 225. Cd. to £2.
The Duke of Gampridge has had the royal
German effrontery to tell a body of Scotch troops —
“ You are fit to do anything—l remember you
serving under me in the Crimean War.” He
didn't remember them scooting back to Hingland
with bogus wounds in their little toes, though.
All the active serving worth mention that fat,
gorgy Georgcy ever did was at the mess dinner-
table. Then lie served himself to 15 sorts of
dessert.
Some little time ago a man named Craven
was arrested in Wellington (M.L.) for stealing an
overcoat from a nail in a public-house. He got
one month, and when he was released from gaol
after working out his sentence, he bolted straight
for the same hotel, and stole the same overcoat a
second time from the same identical nail. This
time he got six mouths. And when lie finishes
the six months, the chances seem about even that
the first thing he will do will be to make a bee-
line for the same pub and steal the same old coat
for the third time. Then he’ll get 12 months, and
after that we trust the coat will be worn out.
THE VERY BEST .
A single pain is often the first indication, and
the last throe, in one, of a physical climax, and
the climax is death. That St. Jacobs Oil has
again and again wrought its miracles just in the
nick of time, has bridged over the critical
emergency, and has
SAVED MANY LIVES
at the point of death, is fully attested by the
record of many extraordinary cures in which this
fact of saving life is duly set forth.
If to its remarkable virtues in the permanent
cure of chronic cases of Rheumatism, Neuralgia,
Lumbago, Sciatica, after years of endurance,
wherein everything else proved valueless, fortunes
were spent in vain, and crutches and crippled
limbs were the manifestations, is to be added its
supreme sovereignty over pain, even unto death,
it is wholly entitled to the appellation of
THE VERY BEST.
Its specific action is especially adapted with a
curative penetration and soothing effect to arrest
any pain at the acme of its worst influence.
A MOST REMARKABLE CASE
can be cited in proof, which is thoroughly verified.
It is in its main features taken from a leading
Liverpool journal as follows: “Two years ago,”
states Mr. William Buchanan, residing at No. 8,
St. John’s-road, Kirkdale, Liverpool, and twenty-
four years in the service of the Cunard Steamship
Co., “ I was taken down with a most excruciat-
ing pain in my head, which was followed by
ONE YEAR OF TORTURE.
All physicians believed that it would lead to
softening of the brain and death. Six of the most
eminent attended me ; but gave no relief. I had
to be held down, and my screams were heard out-
side, My case was deemed incurable. My wife
procured a bottle of St. Jacobs Oil, and applied it
to my head. It acted like magic. I was cured
by it, and am now in perfect health, and I have
not had the slightest return since I was cured
some months ago.” Nothing more need be added.

PROGRESS IN CIVILISATION.
Nature never makes a mistake. Whatever
material is at hand is turned to the best use.
Man, the creation of Nature, follows in her foot-
steps, and steadily seeks a higher plane by re-
jecting the less worthy, and selecting that at his
command which is the most practical. This
progress in civilisation is marked in all details of
life, and particularly in the different apparatus or
mechanical contrivances for every day con-
tingencies. One by one the less fitting drop out,
or are literally crowded aside by those offering
superior advantages. Steam has superseded sail.
The old time horological contrivances are pushed
to the rear by the newer, less costly, and more
reliable appliances of to-day. To-day the public
demand economy, combined with quality,
honesty, and accuracy, and i n all these particulars
the Waterbury watches “ fill the bill,” and there-
by have gained respect and admiration in every
quarter of the earth as honest, reliable, and faith-
ful timekeepers, produced at as low a cost as is
compatible with good material, skilful-workman-
ship, and their undoubtedly excellent qualifi-
cations.

SPORTING NOTIONS.
The latest method of getting the straight tip is
divination by hens. The process is very simple. Get a
Jot of eggs, and mark them with the names of the various
liorses in the Melbourne Cup. Then seize upon the
rooster’s wife and put her on top of them, and wait
results. When the first Ih e chicken comes out, read the
name on the broken shell, and lay your Hide and your
hones on the animal right off. This is as good a way as
any other. The orthodox style, of course, is to have a
seedy-looking tout walk softly in and breathe the name
of a certain horse and a horrid aroma of beer and onions
into your ear, and charge you half-a-orown, hut by the
hen-dodge you get there just the same. Also, you en-
courage a deserving and industrious bird at the same
time, and the fowl knows as much about horse-racing as
you do, anyhow.

A tote-promoter, of King-street, Sydney, sloped
after the Cup. He was hit for the double to the tune of
i!200, but the savant who had invested a shilling with
such splendid results found nothing hut a few empty
cigar-boxes awaiting him when he called at the appointed
hour to receive the ’oof.

Grace had a great reception in Adelaide. The
voyage has added a few stone to his weight, and lie will
have to train down considerably. He is lucky in com-
mencing on the billiard-table wickets of Adelaide.
* # #
George Giffen is in his very best batting form
this year. His marvellous innings against Victoria proves
it. What price a batsman who makes 271, and then takes
the ball and gets 16 wickets for 172 runs in the two
innings, seven of them clean bowled on a splendid wicket?

# * *
Captain Tom Merry has a most interesting
little yarn in the Breeder and Sportsman as to how our
Robinson Crusoe gained his name. According to the
Captain’s yarn, the two-year-old colt by Angler from the
imported Chrysolite was on the City of Melbourne when
she foundered off Coogee in the year 1875, and of the 70
passengers, crew, and some 30 horses, the Chrysolite colt
was the only living thing to reach Coogee beach, when he
was assisted on to dry land by a couple of fishermen, was
appropriately christened Robinson Crusoe, Carried off the
A. J.C. Derby and St. Leger, and when his racing days
were over, begot such animals as Navigator, Trident, and
Sedition to add to his fame. There are one or two little
items in the yarn that are not quite gospel. For instance,
the City of Melbourne is riding at anchor in one of the
hays at Port Jackson at the present time. Joe Thompson,
one of the passengers whom Captain Merry sent to per-
dition off Coogee in ’75, is very much alive in England,
and Robinson Crusoe did wot swim ashore, but was landed
from the City of Melbourne at the old A.S.N. Co.’s wharf
more dead than alive and carted through the Sydney
streets on a trolly. Slight discrepancies of this descrip-
tion don’t count for much in America, however, and tHe
Yanks will enjoy the well-written yarn just as much as if
it were gospel.
# * *
English County cricket does not favour the
proposed Australian eleven, and many of the Counties
have agreed not to play against our men.
Mr. Prince; so well-known as a student of the
thoroughbred, was once upon a time travelling on the
Wollongong (N.S.W.) coach, when one of the passengers
would not give anyone else a chance to get a word in
edgeways. Daddy Hillewell, the basso, was sitting next
to Prince and asked that walking stud-book if be knew
whom the gentleman with the jaw was. “ Ob, yes,” was
the reply, “he is by Talk o’ the Hills, out of Gossip,
named Loquacity.” A bit-further on “ Dad ” commenced
to hum, in his deepest notes,vibe Shakespearian refrain of
“ Who is Sylvia, what is she?” when to his disgust Mr.
Prince gave him in extenso the tabulated pedigree of
Martini-Henry’s famous dam.

The Maoriland horse Maxim is a stallion the
Yankees have long had an eye upon, he being by Musket
from llealization. He has now been purchased by an
American. The Yanks swear 113- Sir Modred and Darebin,
and the performances of their progeny justify it. They

Melbourne Cup.
* * *
The second meeting between Dawson and
Williams, which is fixed for January 25, at Sydney
Amateur Gymnastic Club rooms, may not prove such a
soft thing for Williams as their first fight. Dawson now
knows the rushing style of Williams’ fighting, and must
be aware that the Victorian will not stand off to he
pinked by his scientific left. Dawson’s only chance of
victory is to “ mix it” with his opponent, and then it
remains as to who gets in the first lucky jawbreaker. It
is certainly rather a forlorn hope for a man like Dawson
to change his style of fighting, but it is his only chance.
* * *
Larry Foley gave both gore and science at his
show on Saturday night at the Olympic. Mr. George
Seale and the most scientific heavyweight now in Aus-
tralia gave three rounds, which showed that the “gods ”
do not always thirst for blood if the boxing is really
scientific. Young Sandy Ross knocked Tom Osborne out
of time in the fight to a finish, Osborne showing severe
marks of Sandy’s prowess. Sandy is possessed of the
gluttonous nature of his papa, and although Osborne
landed often and hard, Sandy left off without showing a
scratch.
* * #
Mr. George Glasscock, the well-known rider of
his day, and Melbourne sportsman, died on Saturday last
through being gored by a cow.

* * #
Some Maoriland gluttons are growling because,
during the last five years, Pakehawhaws (copyright) ! ave
sent £26,000 to Sydney turf consultations on the -Mel-
bourne Cup, and have “ only ” received, in prizes, £3700.
Only? Why, over 14 per cent, is a rattling good average
when the tremendous odds necessarily involved in a big
consultation are considered. Thus, only one ticket in
one hundred thousand can pull off the biggest prize in
the (as yet) biggest sweep. Suppose an immortal took
one £1 ticket every year, in a £lOO,OOO sweep, for 100,000
years, he would not even then have readied “level odds ”
for his chance for the first prize of £27,000 (deducting
£3OOO as commission), though he might in the meantime
have pulled off a few minor prizes that made his average
good ; on the other hand, he might have pul'ed off none
at all. Leaving out questions of inti rest on money, his
totalled chances would not ha Lvel, though he would
have raked the run of the serins, tor each frash sweep
renews the odds at the same disadvantage, and because a
man lost last year is no reason why his this year’s shew
is better than 99,909 to 1 ; or, because he won last year,
is no ground tor calculating his chances as worse this
year. Taking interest into consideration in this fancy
problem—£l at s.per cent, compound interest for 100,CCD
years; £2 for 99,999 years; £3 for 99,998 ytars, &c., the
lightning-actuary finds that the gambling-immortal
would have to li\e 100,000 years and spend something
like, 0,985,621,729,731,218 .billions of billions of pounds
sterling, in order to get a square chance of winning
£27,000, with the very open possibility througheut if
winning nothing at all. Add to this, 100,000 post a e-
staraps, and 100,000 registration fees, and 109,000
envelopes, &c., and the interest on the cost thereof, it

Love’s Tontine.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Most love stories have a Mabel, or a Maud, or
Gladys, or Claribel. This one has none of these,
only plain Jeannio Thomson. Not Jinny nor
Jenny, and hardly Thomson ; if trutli is wanted,
she called herself Jeannie Tainsin. She is Scotch.
The heroes, and one was a hero, are not Cyrils,
Cecils, Aubreys, Randolphs, or Mortimers. One
was Jock M’Leish, the other is Wull Scobie.
They were all Scotch, and few Scotchmen are
heroes. lam privileged to say this. I myself am
a Scotchman.
It was Whyte Melville who told the lady that
the extraordinary thing about his most improb-
able and perfectly impossible novel, was that every
word was true. I don’t say my story is true ; but
it is very near it. I don’t like to write it. It is
too horrible, and yet it is not so ghastly as a stab
in the dark or a cowardly murder. It is ghastly
because it is unique. The face of Jock M'Leish
appears when I misshape one of my capitals ;
when a spot of ink falls on the white paper
and spreads into fantasy, when the lamp-
shadow flickers, when the cat mews, when
a coal topples out of the fire; for Jock was
one of my friends, and when I look back I might
have guessed Ids end—but—nothing could turn
M’Leish from an intended line of action.
The story is short. Jock and Wull were fencing
on a small place near llughendeu, Queensland.
There was a new-chum cook, called Jean Thom-
son, and the fencers were allowed to have supper
and breakfast in the kitchen. Jeannie was fair to
sec : one of those healthy Scotch women who come
from about the Ochels, and everyone took a fancy
to her. Both of ourlfencers fell in love with her.
Womanlike, she only fell in love with one of
them, and that was Scobie.
M’Leish was always a morose man ; he did not
drink after the first or second gla-s, but he gam-
bled. He would bet on anything, and ids last two
or three investments were curious and profitable,
but not to him.
The pair of them had a ticket in a sweep, and
drew “Pistol.” We all knew how Pistol won,
but few thought that Scobie and McLeish had
realized T9OO. They never changed their style of
life; but they had only T3OO a-piece, because
they had promised Jeannie a share in the ticket.
Now, the ticket was in John McLeish's name,
and the confidence of the other two was such that
the money was lodged in the bank also entirely in
his name. He said he saw how things were, and
that when the fence was finished he’d settle up
and go away. The other two knew he was deep
gone on the woman. Just about then an insur-
ance-agent came round canvassing for a great
Yankee company. We knew this agent —yellow
face, black teeth, smooth, badly-fitting broadcloth
clothes, dirty nails, and a hideous grin. So he
canvassed for Jock’s life, explaining that if he
were to be shot, or die to morrow, he’d get TICOO,
on payment of £22 yearly. Then Jock asked if
suicides got paid, “Yes, certainly, that’s a
special item in our policies.” He also assured
him that the payments were prompt, and they
went into town, where Jock got a policy for
Til 000, and a guarantee from the local bank-
manager that the affair was square.
Now, in the town of Bungancock, there was, as
in every other country place, a billiard-sharp and
bookie. You can tell them by their unhealthy
pallor and bald head ; also by their diamond ring,
beastly grammar, and profuse obscenity. This
one was a man of “stuff”: he found backers
when he made a big bet and was once known to
own three legs of a horse and to take a contract
wool-washing. Well, he met Jack, and asked him
his business in Bungancork. Life - insurance.
What on earth are you wasting money on that
for ? “ Well,” said jack, “ I believe Will Scobie
will be the means of my death within six months
—at least I’ll take odds about it.”
“ Tell you what I’ll do,” said Spieler-Baldy—
“ I’ll lay you 5 to 1 you don’t die within six months
through the agency, direct or indirect, of William
Scobie.”
“Well, done,” said Jack —“come over to the
bank and post the money.”
If you think of it, this was a real good bet for
the Bald-spieler; and he had a hundred or two
to spare. The manager was induced to place to a
separate account T 250 of Baldy’s money, and £5O
of Jock's. The sum being payable to the order of
both, which was given in a sealed envelope, and
stated the contingency on which it depended.
Then Jock went back to complete his fence.
About a week before the last strainer was put
up he went into town and made his will, and left
it with the local drunkard —I mean, solicitor.
Then came the end of the fence and the end of
Jock. Wull was straining the last wire and talk-
ing to the overseer. Jock was at the further end
and could not be seen for thistles. The wire gave
and gave to the Spanish windlass. Both the over-
seer and Scobie remarked that it took a lot of
turns. So they slacked it and took a new pur-
chase. All right now ; the wire keyed and cut;
the fence is finished ; but where is Jock? “Coo-ee.”
Not an answer ! Then they strolled down to the
next straining-post among the thistles. Crows
have begun to collect. On one side of the fence is
Jock’s head, and on the other side his trunk : cut
asunder by the wire—clean decapitation and to
tiiis day no one can swear how his head got into
the bight of the wire.
But Jeannie is Mrs. Scobie, and the will left
everything to Wull and Jeannie conjointly; and
the Spieler repudiated the bet, but finally paid
by order on the banker : and the insurance-agent,
with tobacco-juice on his lips and mud in his nails,
advised his company to contest it. But being a
new affair, on the contrary it used Jock’s suicide
as an advertisement.
There is a sadness about a homestead lease in
West Queensland ; not engendered by the
natures of the master and mistress, but caused by
the extraordinary way in which the place was
acquired. It is called Tontine Plains.
