The Hermit and the Christian.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Where mighty forest giants rear up, dense, dark,
Their tangled growths to Heaven and make of (ierce
Red summer spring and winter autumn's shades ;
Where from huge mountains, mighty bosoms of
The earth, a thousand sounding waters start
To life and loveliness, and vie in song
With gay-winged birds that sing the whole year round ;
Where, in a word, the forms of earthly things
Are likest to the fabled splendours of
The new-born world his home was, his whom men
Called father, holy one, and reverenced with
Uncovered head. But, oh, how unearned all
This praise ! Three score of years and two had he
Dwelt in this solitude—since manhood’s down
First clothed his cheeks till age his tresses long
And flowing beard had whitened—lived the life
Of man unworthy. Thrice in that long life
Of his th’ invaders trode his native shore,
And steeped in blood the land that gave him birth ;
Yet never raised the hand nor voice in her
Defence, but in his wretched sloth slept on.
Once Famine stalked, a horrid thing, athwart
The land, and he, whom men named holy, raised,
His own wants satisfied, his impious voice
To Heaven and thanked great God the sins of men
Were punished. Such this good man's deeds and of
Like nature all Ids life’s events ; yet, when
He died the monument was wanting not
That told, in carven characters of gold,
The pure and God-like life he led.
But lived
A man, the noblest he of all his race,
Whose highest aim below to follow in
His Master’s, Saviour's, ways was ; city’s slums
He lived and worked in ; nothing for himself
Stored up, but worked from morn till evening for
The good of fellow-men. The criminal
He called his brother ; fallen women of
The streets as sisters loved and cherished—
And all with this reward, that he by those
Whose lives were passed in ease, was spurned and classed
Amongst those fall’n he set himself to raise.
O, what a world of wrongs is this ! Oft has’t
Been said that were the Christ of Nazareth
To come again amongst the men of earth
He would again be wronged to death. And so
It w-as of whom I sing. When sickness bowed
His noble head no comfort was for him,
Nor aid, whose life was spent for all. And so
All unattended passed his spirit away,
Whose life was blessed with deeds of good ; yet none
For him raised up the sculptured tomb to tell
His virtues manifold.
O, say, who should,
Of these two, live forever in the praise
Of men—the one whose life was spent in deeds
Of good, or th’ hermit in his forest-cell?
Yet, not alone in sheltered forest is
The hermit found, but in the roar of cold
Cruel cities. '
JOHN ARUM.
An aboriginal, said to be 103 years of age,
has his quarters ou one of the arms of Middle
Harbour, Sydney. He talks English well, is
good-mannered, and has a stock of old-time tales
to tell. Lately he has been very ill, but is now
again able to potter about his garden.

Tales of Old Sydney.
No. XIV.-For His Child’s Pleasure.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The judgments of early Australian his-
torians (not to say anything as to some later ones)
are seldom to be impugned on the ground that
they are too partial to tbo Powers that Were, but
we must arraign for this crime against historic
truth no less an author than Joseph Holt, some-
time “General” of Irish rebels, and afterwards
“ political transport ” to Botany Bay. When
Holt contented himself with placing Lieutenant-
Colonel Foveaux (at sundry dates and in various
“colonies” representative of Kingly Authority,
and presumably, therefore, instinct with regal
qualities of heart and brain) on a pedestal of
infamy, he misled posterity. He pa'nted Foveaux
as a villain merely. Instead, he should have
pourtrayed him as a monster.
There is a story to the effect that John Mao-
arthur once refused to take Eoveaux’s hand till ho
(the Colonel) had exonerated himself from some
allegation or other —what, has not been recorded,
save that it affected his personal honour. Now,
when Macarthur could repulse his former friend
and tool in that way, imagine, if you can,
Eoveaux’s character.
A pedestal of shame only for the Colonel !
Tush ! He should have had a Bolgia of Dante’s
Hell £.ll to himself !
For he was not merely a wronger of women and
a tyrant of men, a betrayer of his equals’ confi-
dence and a defiler of his inferiors’ homes. Other
gentlemen of Old Sydney shared these distinctions
with him, and there was nothing unique in his
life when tested in relation to these points. His
infamous pre-eminence has its origin in these
facts :
He was the introducer of branding-irons ;
He was a flogger of women ; and
He was a hanger of men without legal form of
accusation or trial.
The tale we tell to-day was not of the first occa-
sion on which Lieutenant-Colonel and Acting-
Governor Foveaux dislocated a man’s neck without
taking the trouble to put in operation the for-
malities British Justice thought necessary when
it desired to complete its work of handing a tran-
sport over to the Devil,
Not the first—by three. That is, as far as our
information goes. At the same time, we are pre-
( jared to concede the possibility that it may not
lave been the first by 13 or 30, as-likely as by
three. Foveaux did not belong to that class of
humorists who exhaust their fancy by a three-fold
repetition of one joke.
# # #
The Colonel was Acting-Governor. It was
during the Interregnum caused by the Rum
Puncheon Rebellion the time which .John
Macarthur aforesaid, was fond of alluding to as
the period of the Commonwealth. And the
Governor ruled in State at Gbvernmint House,
Parramatta.
The beauty of September was on the scene—
the sweet hush, advent-sign of the new spring,
was in the air—the .wattles were becoming
languorous with perfume and colour —the ducks
on the lagoon were bold among the rushes-- aud
His Excellency snored on the verandah, with a
gaily-hued silk-handkerchief covering his face as
he slept recumbent on a “ currency ’’-lounge.*
The cares of .State evidently had lain heavy on
the Governor's mind thus to cause fatigue in the
early afternoon. The caresof .State or, possibly,
the after-effects ’ of the over - night debauch.
Bacchus (to be silent as to Venus) was worshipped
ardently at Government House in Commonwealth
days, and though fine wines were not easily pro-
curable even by gentlemen having access to the
Colonial Treasury, still Bengal rum was cheap.
It was not always an Excellency for the time being
who could afford to consider the refinements of
the palate when weighed against the pocket.
The sentry, a dozen yards away, paced as care-
fully as he could. Nevertheless, a pebble would
sometimes slip under his foot, and he would slide
or stumble,.and already the Governor had stirred
uneasily once or twice. By regulating his steps
and watching where his feet went, the soldier, one
of the historic N.S.W. Corps, hoped tosootheand
not to irritate the great man’s slumber. It was
touching, this solicitude not to disturb His Excel-
lency—but it is open to doubt whether the anxiety
was to preserve his own immunity from punish-
ment, or for the Colonel’s repose.
Ten paces to the right of his box —ten back;
ten thence to the left—ten back ; that was the
sentry’s “ go.” And he thought that what with
measuring his steps, and the fear of disturbing
the Governor, he himself would be in no danger
of falling asleep like that unfortunate sentinel in
Sydney, who a week or two previously had dozed
as he walked, and thus neglecting to receive
Colonel Foveaux with a due salute, had been
cautioned against any future drowsiness by one
thousand lashes.
The balsamic air and the out-pourings from the
cornucopia of sunlight, the monotony of his duty,
and the vacuity of his mind, however, conspired
against the soldier’s wakefulness. His walk
became more mechanical, and his eyes half-closed;
then closed wholly, during one twenty-pace walk;
opened for one second, while he, by force of habit,
turned on the pivot of his heel and retraced his
steps—then closed once more.
He walked so for a few minutes, to all practical
purposes, asleep. He turned at the right-about
spot, machine-like, but, notwithstanding, it was
the case of the Sydney sentry over again.
Even to the thousand lashes, it was the same
case.
* « «
He must have been asleep, else he would
have heard the boisterous accents of a man coming
up the ascent from the park-gates—past the ruins
of the first Rosehill cottages. 'The fellow
was gay to the Verge of indecorum, for he now
waved his cabbage-tree hat in the air, and now
flapped it against his sides, in sheer recklessness
of movement, while lie poured forth, in tuneless
volume, one of the “ flash ” songs which were as
popular among the lower-class “ free ” as among
the transports for whose edification they had been
originally composed.
If the voice was unmelodious the spirit was
joyous, and the words were by “flash” standard,
choice : //
Come, Bet, my pet,' and Sal, my pal, a buss and then
farewell;
And, Ned, the primest ruffing cove that ever nail’d a
swell— , . -
To shaije the swag, or chaff the gab, we'll never meet
again, •
The hulks is now my browsing crib, the hold my dossing
ken.
“The hold my doss—my doss-ing—k-ken !” echoed
bravely. Admirable was the attempt to impart
expression, and to appropriately convey the
heroic sentiment which the poet—no doubt one of
Jemmy Catnach’s high-quality hacks—had put
into the mouth of an embryo colonist. Had the
singer been aware that he had for audience the
distinguished head of the colony himself, he could
not have sung in a more encore-provoking fashion.
Perhaps, indeed, he would not have trolled forth
so heartily. The proximity of Governor Eoveaux’s
person was not a circumstance calculated to
encourage warmth of feelings of a pleas-
ing kind—especially when the Colonel had
been suddenly roused by undignified sounds
and had perceived in the first opening of
his own eyelids that the sentry’s were stupified by
somnolence though he was moving in his pen-
dulum-swing.
Without rising from the lounge, the Governor
roared : “ Halt! ”
The sentry was startled into wakefulness —and
an indiscretion. He turned and faced the veran-
dah, lifting in the same instant Ins disengaged
hand to his eyes as though to rub them. No
clearer admission could anyone desire than that
he had been overcome by drowsiness.
“Asleep on your post! By ,my man ”
given somewhat to forcible speech was his Excel-
lency—“ you haven’t learnt anything from that
last week’s flogging ! And who are you, you fellow?
How dare you, sir, come here ? ”
The question was addressed to the singer who,
still chanting his verse—
Don’t nab the bib, my Bet, this chance must happen soon
or later,
For certain sure am I that transportation comes by natur ;
His Lordship’s self, upon the Bench, so downey his white
w ig in
Might sail with me, if friends had he to bring him up to
' priggin’—
had come into sight through the hedge which
divided the house-garden—Governor Phillips’s
“crescent” —from the park-land.
“ To prig-gin’— to prig-gin’ ” —he dwelt impres-
sively on the final notes, and was not very much
surprised when the Governor, on hearing whose
voice he had, of course, paused in his song as well
as in his walk, ordered him to “sing that over
again.” No vocalist yet was ever surprised at
a request for an encore.
“Go on! That’s not the end, is it?” the
Representative of Royalty was pleased to say as
the possibility of his Lordship’s fate had his early
training been different was once more musically
expressed. And, the singer having first saluted
he knew his manners, and as a veteran of the Old
Corps, of course, he showid respect in the manner
of his former profession—favoured his laughing
Excellency, and the trembling sentry, and the
stupefied sergeant and man of the guard with the
last stave ;
And is it not inkimmon fly on them as rules the nation,
To make us end with Botany, our public edication ?
But Sal, so kind, be sure you find, the beaks don’t catch
you trippin’,
You’ll find it hard to be for shoppm’ sent on board th
shippin’!
“Sent on bo-oard th’ shippin’—th’ shippin’.”
“ Bravo ! Bravo ! ” His Excellency applauded
heartily. “ Well sung ! But isn’t there a chorus?”
“ Oh, yes, y’r Honor! I was a-forgettin’ th’
chorus. I'll tip it yer, sir.”
And with a twirl of his cabbage-tree, he shouted
-—“Chorus”—
So tip your manns, afore we part, don’t blear your eyes
and uose-es, ...
Another grip, my jolly hearts -here’s luck, and on wi
goes-es!
‘‘Good, again!’’ The Governor was genuinely
affable in his appreciation of the vocal artist.
“And ’tis quite true— ’tis a case of ‘Off wo
gocs-es.’ Sergeant, lock-up that drunken fellow
for creating a disturbance in Government 1 louse
grounds, and yon sentry for permitting him and
for being asleep on his post ! By ——, I’ll make
an example of both of ’em, disturbing me this
way. Off with 'em both to the guard-room !’
The sentry made no resistance; he was dis-
armed and led awty passively, for he had come
to a realisation of tne fata that was in store for
him, and had resolved to meet it valiantly. The
man of the Sydney Company had not .cried out
chop during the terrible inttic ion of his thousand,
and if lie could help it he would not during his
lot.
With the singer, however, the case was different.
Although he was an ex-soldier, he was smitten
with tire desperation of fear. Variously, Foveaux
was termed by'the men of - the Corps, “Bloody
Toveaux ” and “Tiger Toveaux,” and each appella-
tion was a testimony to the almost insatiable
bloodthirstiness of the officer when his appetite
had once been whetted by tasting the droppings
of the scourger’s cats. This is a figure of speech
which yet is not a figure merely. The meaning
hereof may be told some day.
The singer, paid out of the Corps these five
years, had not forgotten the Colonel’s reputation,
and ho had rejoiced in his heart that the great
man was in so pleasant a mood that day, as evi-
denced by his desire to hear the conclusion of the
song, for it augured well, he had thought, for the
granting of the request he had come all the way
from Sydney to prefer to his Excellency—which
was that his “right tooceupy” might be made into
a grant—so that he could sell his land. Now, the
re-action of feeling shocked him out of his manli-
ness, and he cringed before the Acting-Governor.
“Oh, y’r Honor, not me, sir! I ain’t dun
enythink, y’r Excellency. An’ his true I’ve had
a ball+ of rum, sir, but I ain't in no manner of
bein’ drunk, sir !”
“ To the guard-room, sergeant !”
“ No, y’r Excellency, surely not!” the poor
sinner, sickened by his extremity, pleaded. “Not
without a-hearin’ of me, sir, an’ why I’m here.
I’m hero with a letter, sir, from the Secretary,
y’r Excellency, Secretary Blaxall ’tis urgent,
y’r Honor!’’
“What does he say ?” demanded the magnani-
mous Head of the State. “He has a letter ? ’
“Yes, y'r Honor—y’r Excellency, I mean, sir.”
“Unhand him, sergeant, till he gives you the
letter!” -- '
Treed from the clutch of the guard for n minute,
the wretched offender against the vice-regal
dignity pulled from the folds of his dungaree shirt
a folded and scaled packet.
“This is it, y’r Excellency—from Carnham
Blaxall, Es-quire, Acting-Colonial Secretary. Tis
in support, sir, of my application.’’ His craven-
heartedness was gradually going away before the
rush of returning hope,
“ Pass me the letter, sergeant—the man’s appli-
cation can wait till I hear his case to-morrow.”
Back into its terrors went the poor worm on
whom Authority had placed its righteous heel.
“ Oh, no, y’r Excellency; now please—hear me !
To-morrow’ll be too late !”
“Too late! Too late ! Are you Governor, or
am I?” shouted his Excellency. “ Too late! By
, ’tis never too late to refuse an application !
Your application is refused ! Off with him, ser-
geant, I say.” - „

“Oh, y’r Excellency—don’t say that—.” He
wrested himself from the grasp of the privates
who had held him, and rushed towards the Gover-
nor, who was standing just at the head of the
steps leading to the piazza. Eoveaux, the arrant
coward that he was, started back a couple of paces.
j,ike most tyrants, he was in constant morbid
fear of personal attacks, and, indeed, it is a won-
der that no more than nine or ten assaults upon
his scoundrel’s body are recorded.
On this occasion, however, his alarm was need-
less. The man stopped, respectfully enough, at
the foot of the steps to plead his cause.
“ Look, y’r Excellency—l’m due for my grant a
year ago —if I had it now I could sell it and get
home— there’s a ship sails next week—the first for
two months—and I want to get home by her, me
and my wife—we must be in Lunnun on a certain
day, or else I lose a pot o’ money, y’r Excellency—
ancl the notice of goin’ away X can then be published
in the paper on Sunday, if I get y’r Honor's, y’r
Excellency's, grant to-day !”
Once more tlie blasphemous oath fell from Vice-
regal lips, ,
“By G- —sergeant! I’ve a mind to tickle
you and your men for permitting this blackguard
to break arrest.”
Urged by this remonstrance, the guard rusned
forward—the sergeant, the corporal, and three
men. There Was a struggle, a mighty struggle,
John Lea, ex-member of the New South Wales
Corps, understood now that he Was fighting fot
What Was more to him then than life—his freedom.
Re threw the sergeant on his back, and tripped
the corporal so neatly that he fell upon his pros-
trate brothet-non.-com. The privates bad held
back for a second to enable their officers to get a
grip Upon the man, but now they, too, propelled
themselves upon him. Before, however, they could
beat him down, he had retreated up the steps, the
path to the gate being closed by his assailants;
Tlie men followed him. The foremost went down
mucker than he came up, and in his descent
received oh his forehead a gash from the kerb of
the lowest step. The others were more successful —
they clenched Lea in their grasp and, after one
ihtensespasmof heroic effort, heabandoned himself
to theit clutch. Not, however, till he had perpe-
trated a terrible-, a dastardly crime. He struck the
governor—the Representative of Royalty—-the
Anointed Of John Macarthur! Eoveaux, at Lea’s
rUsh Up the steps, experienced that sensation of
patalysis which seizes upoh the bully-coward at
the approach Of danger. He stood s‘ark-still, and
so came easily Within the sweep of John Lea’s
fight arm, as, in the violence of the struggle with
the guard, he flung it out. His Excellency re-
coiled to the house-wall in actual pain, so forcible
was the blow which had fallen upon his face.
There was at least one onlooker who averred to
his dying day that the blow, so far as the receipt
by the Governor was concerned, was accidental.
If we believed that were so, we candidly confess
that we should leave the tragic close of J ohn Lea’s
life unchronicled. The man who, treated as Lea
was, and having the opportunity to strike his
tyrant, consciously withheld his hand, deserved
his doom.
Which was—death Death mstanter, on the
R P ot * ; •
Just beyond the angle of the liouSe-gardell,
in the park-land, but so close that a gadding
tenril from the vine within the angle pushed a
tender point into contact with its barkless bole,
stood a great Eucalyptus. One of its many stalwart
arms afforded to the connoisseur in the apparatus
of hanging satisfactory evidence that Nature
could be depended upon to furnish as complete a
gallows equipment as could be desired even by a
finished gentleman of Old Sydney. It was sound
and of ample length, and sprang from the trunk
at a sufficiently-low elevation to enable the rope
to be thrown round it without much difficulty,
while sufficiently high to supply the desideratum
of an effective neck-breakage, or strangulation—
the latter preferred.
To the foot of this tree, about half-an-hour after
Ex-private John Lea had occasioned Governor
Eoveaux some sudden pain, that heinous criminal
was haled by some fore-time comrades of the Rum-
selling Corps.
Governor Eoveaux would, when recollection and
Coolness of judgment returned to him, refer, as
his authority for the proceeding, to an eatly-day
regulation permitting ihstant execution for “overt
mutiny.” That regulation Was, of course, obso-
lete, otherwise Macarthur, Johnston, and Eoveaux
Would have been hanged not many months before.
Nevertheless it now served, or rather Would have
served, to hang John Lea had the Acting-Governor
only thought of it. Failing to recall it just at the
time, he procured his warrant from another
equally potential source—the maddened temper of
that infuriated bully, himself.
“ You won’t hang me in the sight of
the condemned man gasped in the Sergeant’s ear,
as he was pulled along from the guard-room to the
tree; ,
“The house?” The non-commissioned officer
ttot knowing what Left alluded to, filled up the
gap in the broken speech to suit his oWn fancy,
“ Oh, the old man won’t mind ! Damn it, Lea,
you hit me hard, but I’m sorry for you ! ”
“The house ! What do I care for the house —of
the old man—him—or you ! Are you men to hang
ine with them there ? ”
His arms were pinioned to his side—handcuffs;
pinioning cord and one-irich rope were always
available at the shortest notice at evety official
establishment in Old Sydney, whatever other
stores were lacking—and what he meant with his
hand he was thus unable to indicate. But he
jerked his head in the direction of the lower-park.
The Sergeant followed the index-sign with a
glance, and saw nothing more than a woman sit-
ting on the grass a few hundred yards away with
a child gambolling about her.
“Oh, that doesn’t matter! Never mind ’im,
Lea, but say a prayer for yourself.”
“ Don’t ye understand ”
“ Blindfold him there !” cried His Excellency,
who was not above attending to trifling details of
formality. “And bring out that sentry under
arrest. He shall hang him ! ”
In the jvrithings of the poor wretch, the word B
that fell from his lips, on which the death-ooze wa s
already visible, must have been unheeded by the
Sergeant and the other soldiers of the fatal escort,
or surely they had not been so contaminated by
the example of their superior officers as to be quite
insensate to the enormity they wereabouttocommit.
They had, s ine of them, been the condemned
man’s messmates, had fed out of the same dish,
from the same loaf. Surely, then, they would not
have been participators in that sacreligious out-
rage on the holiest of human shrines? They had
not the excuse, flimsy, but still an excuse, which
Eoveaux could offer, for thev had not been
devilised by the exercise of unbridled power.
Surely they did not hear ?
But though the escort did not heed the Con-
demned’s words, the disgraced sentry, who had
emerged under guard from the cell by the guard-
room, and been dragged into Eoveaux’s presence,
did.
“ Hang that man ! ” exclaimed the Authority,
“ and I’ll free you from arrest! ”
The soldier sickened. But—the drummer’s cats
while they were playing the first three or four
hundred of his thousand § would hurt acutely—
they would cut worse even than the three-dozen
he had once received —and someone would have
to haul on the rope. And why not he then ?
He consented —saluted —was marched over to
the tree —and laid hands on ex-Private Lea.
That was the beginning and the end of his per-
formance of an executioner’s duties. Bending
down to bind Lea’s legs he caught the meaning
of the dying man’s blurted phrase, and—the devil
Was in the silly fellow certainly—-drawing himself
to his full height, he gave one glance towards the
resting woman and the playing child, and threw
Up his billet.
“ I’ll take the flogging, sergeant!” he croaked.
“ If it kills me—well, the good God won’t forget
I didn’t do that in the sight of them”
All the sattie, as he retired under arrest, with
another three hundred added to his emoluments,
someone was found to take his place. That some
one soon put the finishing touch to ex-Private
Lea, grantee under “Discharged- Soldiers’Regu-
lations ” of fifty acres on the Hawkesbury, who,
could he have presented himself in London by a
certain day six months distant, would have come
into a small fortune. And now
# * ' *
“ Where is dada, muzzer?” had questioned
the little child, a girl of five. “ I’s gettin’ tired,
an’ wants to do home.” ( '
The woman, also somewhat Wearied with a
waiting now an hour long, called the child to her
lap from the verdant sward, tesselated With
wild flowers, where she had been gambolling,
and soothed her with sweet, silly mother’s talk;
“ Dada’ll be here soon, Polly—he’s gone to the
big house yonder to see the Governor. And then
we’ll go home. ”
“An’ will dada bwing me sweeties, muzzer—
you said I s’u’d have some ?”‘
“ So, dear, you shall, bye-and-bye —lots of
sweeties. And then, when we go ’cross the big,
big sea, in the big, big boat, oh, we shall have
such lots of money to buy sweeties for my little
girl with!”
The child turned in' her mother’s lap, in the
restlessness of an over-tired frame, and grew
querulous and tearful.
“ Oh, me do wish dada’d come—l’s so tired !”
“ Hush, dearie, —he will come soon—p’r’aps the
Governor’s busy, and dada can’t see him ”
The tiny tot, nestling against her mother’s right
shoulder, was looking up the hill, and interrupted
her mother by eager, curious exclamations.
“Muzzer, muzzer! Look! See what they’s
doin’! Oh, isn’t that funny, muzzer 1”
She did not feel her weariness now—that
strange thing dangling fantastically, with every
movement made clearly visible by the cloudless
ether, amused her immensely. She stood up, and
laughed, and clapped her hands.
The woman turned her head at the child’
enquiry, and then hastily interposed herself
between the spectacle on the crest of the eminence
and the little girl’s sight. The child did not
understand, she thought!
And did she—then? Not till later. When
they gave her the body.

“Really!”
“If you were to drop ‘ Oh! ’ and * Really ? ’ out
of the English language, the major part of tae
British aristocracy would be destitute of con ver-
sation.”
Such was the remark an American friend made
to me the other c'a •. We were discussing the
question referred to in a recent newspaper letter
—the alleged inferiority of American men to
Englishmen in polish, refinement, social accom-
plishments, general information, and conversa-
tional powers. I had to laugh, it seemed so very
true.
“ I’m afraid I’ll have to agree with you,” I
said ; “ I’ve often thought the same thing mys* If.
And if you’d bar the interrogatives, ‘ Did you ?’
‘Were you?’ ‘ Was it?’ ‘Did it?’ ‘Does it?’ ‘ls
he ?’ etc., spoken with a labored emphasis outlie
first word, in reply to every remark or suggestion
yon make, nine-tenths of them would have to sit
silent.”
“Precisely. So they would. There’s one other
word, when I come to think of it, which wo may
leave out, too ; it’s 1 Fancy ! ’ Now, I think we’ve
collared the swell’s vocabulary. Deprive the
swells of these words and the stereotyped inter-
rogations you suggest, and, so far as they were
concerned, silence would reign supreme. Take an
English country-house. For example, the break-
fast-table. These breakfasts are all alike. I’ve
been present at dozens, in as m.yny different houses,
and one description will fit all; the same chill
atmosphere of male gruffness and female rigidity
permeates each. Now, it’s a curious thing that,
the other day, I was thinking about this thing,
and hastily scratched down some notes and im-
pressions from recollection. I’ve got ’em in my
pocket-book, and, if you don’t mind, I’ll read
them to you.”
So he began :
SCENE.
Breakfast-room at Beaulieu Manor. High wains-
cot of old oak ; walls papered in deep maroon ;
deep maroon damask window-curtains , and
maroon, leather-seated chairs. Old oak fire-
place ; log-fire in the grate ; long breakfast-
table, hissing urn and tea-things at one end,
four covered silver dishes at the other, containing
cutlets, sausages, poached eggs, and curried
fowl. In the middle and up the sides, plates of
hot rolls in napkins; a large dish of butter
scrolls and bullets, a silver stand of boiled eggs,
a glass dish of orange marmalade, and two
racks of dry toast. On sideboard, cold ham,
beef, game, and huge loaf of bread.
PEOPLE AT TABLE.
Lady Bab-Dexter (the lady of the house) aged
thirty-five, once pretty, now buxom, with that
burnt-faced, diminishing-eyed look which the
average high-born British matron (unless a
“ frisky ') gets in a few years after marriage,
and is not so much the result of annual mater-
nity as the effect of an unlimited consumption
ofbroivn stout at luncheon and brown sherry
at dinner.
The Hon. Mbs. Yilliers and Miss Villiebs,
mother and daughter. Mother, grey-haired,
arched eye-brows, pale, thin, and icy ; daugh-
ter, thorough-bred and shy.
Lady Violet Cbopper, a “frisky"; pretty,
bold, cold-eyed, and horsey.
Lobd Henby’Noddle, her brother.
Captain Fitzrubbishe, of the Queens Own Bom-
bardiers.
[Silence reigns. Enter your humble servant —
whom we will call Mb. Thompson Witha-
pee, of Philadelphia. Both the men are
reading their letters while they eat, the torn-
open envelopes littering the table and adjoin-
ing plates. ]
Men— “ Bawl” [which I interpret as “Good-
morning. ”]
Women— “Ning !” [which I ditto'].
[I seat myself in one of a half-dozen vacant places
amid utter silence. After a pause :]
Lady Bar-Dexter— “Tea, Mr. Withapee?”
I—“ If you please.”
[Lady 8.-D. pours out the tea and I wait some
minutes.]
Lady Bar-Dexter —‘ Here is your tea, Mr.
Withapee. [/ am separated from her ladyship by
Noddle and Fitzbubbishe, but neither offers to
pass the cup.] “Come and get it, please.” [This
I discover to be the custom. Every one gets up and
goes for his own tea. Igo hack to my seat and won-
der hoiv I shall get something to eat. While I sip
my tea and puzzle about it:]
Lady Bab-Dexter —“TheHammonds come to-
morrow, Captain Fitzrubbishe.”
Captain Fitzrubbishe— “ Oh ! Do they ? ”
Lady Bab-Dexter —“ They can only stay two
nights, though.”
Captain Fitzrubbishe— “ Really. Can’t they?”
[Enter Lord Basil Dumplinge, age twenty-five, in
scarlet hunting-coat and top-boots.]
Men— “ Baw ! ”
Women— “ ’Ning ! ”
[Dumplinge makes straight for the silver dishes,
lifts the cover off each, aud scrutinises contents
through eye-glass. Looks disappointed, but
helm himself to a poached egg, and carries it to
seat next me. Sits down and prroceeds to open
his letters, which are in a pile beside his plate.
I take the tip, and go and help myself to a
sausage.]
Lord Basil Dumplinge [with eyes on letter] —
“By Jove ! I say ” —[to Lady Violet Cropper,
to whom he hasn’t before spoken.]
Lady Violet— “ Hello ! ”
Lord Basil— “ Here’s a lark! The Jones-
Fieldings have a meet at their shop next
Tuesday.”
Lady Violet—" Never ! ”
[Lord Basil tears open another letter with his
thumb.]
Captain Fitzrubbishe—“ Really !”
Lady Bar-Dexter[<oMissVilliers]— “There’s
to be a hunt-ball at Boskell next week.”
Miss Villiers—“ /s there ? ’
[Enter Sib John Bab-Dexter, a bearded man of
forty-five, and a bluff manner, also in hunt-
ing “pink.’’]
Men —“ Baw I”
Women —“ ’Ning!”
Sir John [after helping himself in silence to
some cold grouse from the sideboard] —“Look
sharp, Dumplinge. Ha’-pas’ nine and eight miles
to Tombridge Tun.”
Lady Violet— “ Going to ride Vixie?”
Lord Basil—" No fear.”
[7 have disposed of my sausage and think Ell say
something.]
I—“ What a beautiful view there is from my
room window, Lady Bar-Dexter.”
Lady Bar-Dexter —“ Oh, is there ?”
I —“It is the finest woodland scenery I can
remember.”
Lady Bar-Dexter—“Really, /sit?”
I—“ Yes. It seemed like a reproduction of one
of Wilkie’s or Birket Foster’s best landscapes.”
Lady Bab-Dexter— “ Fancy !”
The other men look up and regard me curiously
through their eyeglasses. Lady V iolet winks
openly at Dumplinge, who draws down the
corners of his mouth. I feel sat upon, and
subside .]
Sir John— “ Ought to have a rattling good run
to-day. My tea, please.”
[And so on for half-an-hour longer, while three or
four more men come in, and I sit and listen.]
“COCKAIGNE,” IN THE ARGONAUT.
p
hack to the nearest station on the tableland.
During the evening, the coroner commenced dis-
coursing, in an imaginative manner, upon the
manner of the lonely death, and the weirdness of
the locality. This sort of thing was laughed at by
the squatter, who offered, for a bet of <£5,
to ride to the spring and back before mid-
night. The question arose as to how the
coroner should know that the ride had been
duly performed. The constable settled the ques-
tion by remarking that the dead swagman’s pipe,
knife, and tobacco were lying on a stone beside
the remains of the fire. However, the wager
didn’t come off. The squatter happened to be
down at the men's hut the following evening ;
noticing an old swagman sitting by the fire, he
enquired : “ Do you want any tobacco, old man ?”
“ No, thanks ; I made a rise where I camped last
night. Some carrier left his knife, pipe, and
tobacco on a stone, and made a big heap of earth
—what for, I couldn’t make out. Anyhow, it
served me for a pillow.” Now, had the bet been
made, and the squatter come cantering round the
sharp turn in the road before reaching the spring,
it is just possible that time would have been too
precious for mutual enquiries. The old fellow
might have been travelling towards the coast the
next day, and the district would have had a brand-
new ghost.
Queenslanders must have large heads ! The

FEDERATION OR NO FEDERATION.
The latest scheme for Federation
Claims to be for the country’s weal,
While all may prove but botheration,
Which can’t be said of Pemell’s Meal.
His Golden Meal is what I mean,
’Tis good and pure and cheap I ween.
Another precious Adelaide “saint” has
gone on the broad path, accompanied by a large
amount of loose cash. He worked the impression-
able Adelaide people in the usual way. He came
from—well, abroad ; and from what has been said,
he came in a hurry. He soon found out the best
way to make a living in Adelaide —made a
bee-line for one of the churches, where he speedily
became an official. At holy meetings and sacred
lectures he was all there, and at praying he was a
splendid grafter, fervency being bis speciality.
He was the best hand ever known in the sacred
city at an impromptu prayer, barring Crooks, the
felonious bank manager, and the gaoled Walker,
ex-Adelaide Y.M.C.A. secretary. And now
he is missing, like the others w'ero, and his till is
empty, and all is desolation.
Many ridiculously high-flown paragraphs
are going the rounds concerning Holt, now within
the seclusion of Darlinghurst prison for forgery.
The facts are that Holt was a most commonplace
swindler, and it must irritate many worthy people
who have had dealings with him to hear the
fellow exalted as a distressed British “nobleman,”
and spoken of as “coming from good stock.” &c.
“The Majors ” distinguished chuck-a-chest mili-
tary air was acquired from the master for whom
he performed the duties of valet, though he him-
self ascribed it to the fact that he had been
equerry to the Duke of Connaught, no less. His
figure (if he did not wear stays he was the kind of
man who would) was well-known in the lunch-
hour on the Sydney Block, where he was much
admired —by little shop-girls—but his best effort
was the wheedling of £200 out of a weak, confiding
teacher of singing—the amount was handed to
him to invest in a safe and profitable venture
recommended by “TheMajor.” There were tech-
nical difficulties in the way of a prosecution, other-
wise this man of pedigree would have ceased
to bilk landladies some years ago. The newspapers
give out that he “ occupied a responsible position
in the city,” which being interpreted means that
he formerly presided over “ the carpets ” in a
retail softgoods shop. Latterly he ran a matri-
monial agency in Melbourne and Sydney, but
without much success, as the game is almost
played out. But beyond all doubt he for some
years pervaded Sydney as the quintessence of a
fifth-rate British snob, and on Ids trial the other
day a count should have been added to the indict-
ment charging him with that offence.

Somebody—a hopeless lunatic apparently
—writes to the Sydney Echo : —
Legislation has assumed the burlesque type. In order
to reduce as far as possible the amount of mischief done
by Parliaments, I think that it is desirable that all action
not completed between 6 a.m. and 12 p.m. (midnight)
shall be illegal; also, that injuries inflicted shall be
punishable by the exercise of Governmental authority
and that injuries inflicted wilfully shall ho punishable by
Crown prosecution, even flogging being resorted to in
cases of flagrant embezzlements, wife-beating, &c. jj.
object is to protect the weak from brutal attacks. 3 |
The address of the asylum the writer escaped out
of isn’t given. Why didn’t the Echo admit
letters from this variety of maniac when the
Mount Rennie prisoners were being tried at four
o'clock in the morning? That seems to come
under the head of “all action not completed
between G a.m. and 12 p.m. (midnight).”
